==PAGE:4==
Abstract
The 4-f line pulse shaper operates by manipulating the phase of laser
pulses in the frequency domain. This opens the door to time-resolved
control of molecular reactions via shaped femtosecond pulses, and thereby
the ability to drive systems into thermodynamically unfavorable states.
The complex relationship between the spectral phase chirp and temporal
pulse shape means it is important to develop principles that help relate
these concepts, and to devise a framework for using this information to
further the understanding of quantum control on the femtosecond time-
scale.
This thesis provides analytic derivations to the reverse Fourier transforms
and Wigner distributions of a series of spectral chirps, in particular, the
cubic polynomial and π step functions.
Additionally, time-dependent analytic solutions to a generalized Gaussian
quantum wave packet propagating in a forced harmonic oscillator are de-
rived.
A genetic algorithm is used to optimize simulated laser pulses interacting
with simple quantum OH molecule and double well potential models in
the semiclassical limit. It is demonstrated that applying optimized spec-
tral chirps to shape a 10 femtosecond FWMH, 23 J/cm2 Gaussian pulse
can increase the dissociation of the OH ground state by ﬁve orders of
magnitude from ∼0.0003% to ∼45%. Using the same methodology, it
is then shown that state population transfer in the double well can be
increased by over 18 orders of magnitude.
Finally, through investigation of optimized pulse characteristics, it is demon-
strated that the dynamics of optimal population transfer in the quantum
double well are independent of the driving pulse temporal width, once a
minimum temporal width threshold has been reached.

Part I
Introduction

==PAGE:12==
Chapter 1
Introduction
Imagine being able to assume direct control of a chemical reaction, deciding exactly
which bonds to break and which bonds to make, enabling the construction of molecu-
lar conﬁgurations that have never been seen before in nature. It’s a tantalizing dream,
and since the advent of the laser in the 1960s, large steps have been taken towards
this dream becoming a reality.
The motion of atoms at the molecular scale is on the order of ≈1 kilometer per
second [41], and the characteristic length scale over which this motion occurs on is
the order of a few Angströms, or ten-billions of a meter. Therefore, the timescale in
which a molecular reaction occurs is on the order of a few to a few hundred million-
billionths of a second, or femtoseconds. The burgeoning ﬁeld of femtochemistry is the
study of reactions on this timescale.
Energy is required to drive chemical reactions, and is normally supplied in the
form of heat or light. These methods are statistical in nature; they inﬂuence global
parameters of the system and the focus is on the end products of the reaction. Fem-
tochemistry diﬀers in this respect as it involves directly controlling or probing the
evolution of a system “in real time”. In order for this to be achieved, it is necessary
to develop tools and techniques that also operate on the femtosecond timescale.
2
==PAGE:13==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
1.1
A Brief History
Over the past century, the time resolution at which chemical reactions can be studied
has increased dramatically.
• The study of reaction rates led to the Arrhenius equation [22] in 1889, which
relates temperature to chemical reaction rate.
• In 1923, the ﬁrst direct sub-second resolution measurements of chemical reaction
velocities were made possible when H. Hartridge et al designed an experiment
in which reactants were mixed in high velocity ﬂow tubes, and the reaction was
observed at various spatial intervals along a ﬂow channel [13], achieving time
resolution on a scale of tens of milliseconds.
• Around 1950, time resolution at the microsecond timescale was achieved when
R. G. W. Norrish and George Porter developed the method of ﬂash photolysis,
in which a brief intense ﬂash of light is used to disturb the equilibrium of a
sample, after which a series of secondary light pulses probe the system and
record the spectra of the intermediate states.
• Around 1954, Manfred Eigen was able to achieve micro- and even nano- second
time resolutions through development of the relaxation method [22, 7] which in-
volves disturbing the equilibrium of a system via sudden change in temperature,
pressure, or electric ﬁeld, and following the kinetics as the system equilibrates.
At the time, it was widely thought that the last two techniques on this list represented
the absolute lower limit of achievable time resolution. This quickly changed after the
ﬁrst laser was developed by Theodore Maiman in 1960. By 1966, lasers were able to
generate short bursts of coherent light that were only a few picoseconds long, and a
few decades later, it became possible to create light pulses of just a few femtoseconds.
[2]. Ahmed Zewail et al. started working on an experiment at Caltech in 1985 with
the goal of monitoring the bond breakage (ICN ∗→I +CN) [41], in which a “pump”
3
==PAGE:14==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
pulse was used to dissociate the molecule, after which the reaction kinetics of the
system were probed using a series of femtosecond pulses at varying time intervals.
Two years later, a time resolution of ≈40 [fs] was achieved, leading to the ﬁrst ever
time-resolved measurements of the transition states in a chemical bond.
Lasers opened the door to monitoring chemical reactions with ever increasing
time resolution, and today it is possible to generate pulses all the way down to the
attosecond time scale [19]. In addition to this, lasers oﬀer the possibility of being able
to control the system dynamics on similar time scales through coupling of the electric
ﬁeld to the molecular dipole moment. In 1980, Zewail published a paper titled “Laser
Selective Chemistry - is it Possible?”
[40] in which the feasibility of using tuned
laser pulses to drive molecular systems into thermodynamically unfavorable states
was explored.
The introduction of the 4f-line spectral pulse shaper by C. Froehly et al. in 1983
[5], coupled with the development of controllable liquid crystal phase masks, led to
the ability to create and tailor complex femtosecond laser pulse shapes with features
that evolve on the same characteristic time scales as the molecular systems. This is
one of the main jumping-oﬀpoints of the theoretical analysis of this thesis. In chapter
2, it will be described how this system has been implemented using open and closed
looped control schemes in a variety of diﬀerent experimental situations.
1.2
Main Thesis Objectives
Brieﬂy stated, the main objectives of this thesis are:
• A study of laser pulse shaping with the aim of deriving electric ﬁeld expressions
for some simple pulse shapes
• Application of these laser pulses in order to control the dynamics in a simple
model of a molecule
4
==PAGE:15==
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
Inspired by these objectives, this thesis will attempt to cover as much ground as
possible. The following section will serve as form of road map to highlight the most
important points.
1.3
Structural Outline of Thesis
Many of the ideas explored in the following pages are described “from the ground
up”. Therefore, as is often the case with any thorough investigation, this thesis will
consist of a blend of “old” and “new” material; The reader with knowledge of the ﬁeld
is likely to have encountered many of these concepts before, and (hopefully) some
territory that is less familiar to the reader will be covered as well. At the risk of
being presumptuous, the “less common” material in the following overview is printed
in blue font for reference. This is not an assertion that these ideas or results cannot
be found elsewhere, but rather that they are, to the authors present knowledge, not
things that are often presented in the standard literature.
What follows is a general overview of the content and structure of the six parts of
this thesis. Reading the descriptions under the part headers is suﬃcient to understand
the general structure of the thesis, and reading the descriptions under the chapter
headings will provide more detailed explanations of the content.
Part I: Introduction
Contains a brief introduction to the ﬁeld of femtochemistry and the general content
and overview of this thesis
Chapter 1: Overview
• Key concepts and results pertaining to the ﬁeld of femtochemistry
• The objectives of this thesis

chirp. A variety of spectral phase chirps are investigated using numerical and analyt-

The genetic algorithm is introduced as a method of optimization. It is demonstrated
that the genetic algorithm can optimize a spectral chirp function to produce a desired
temporal pulse shape. It is demonstrated that the genetic algorithm can be used
to optimize a temporal frequency function, and a spectral phase chirp function, to
maximize the dissociation probability of the OH Morse potential ground state, and
the population transfer of a bistable double well potential.

1.4
A Note About Code
A signiﬁcant portion of the work done in this thesis has involved computer simulations.
All code was written in Matlab, and the only pre-written software packages that have
been employed are those from the standard Matlab library (i.e. the ﬀt () and ga ()
functions). All other functions and scripts have been coded from the ground up, and
can be found, with brief explanations, in appendix M. Whenever relevant, thorough
descriptions of the important Matlab functions have been included as well.

Part II
Pulse Shaping
11
==PAGE:22==
Chapter 2
Pulse Shaping in Practice
2.1
Introduction
This chapter will brieﬂy introduce the general concepts behind the construction and
deployment of experimental pulse shapers. This will generate perspective and moti-
vate the theoretical analyses that follows in the body of this thesis. Speaking in very
general terms, the concept behind a pulse shaper is to transform an incoming laser
pulse into a desired shape. For ultrashort pulses on the femtosecond time scale, this
pulse shaping is most often accomplished in the spectral domain.
2.2
The 4f Line
This thesis will be focusing the method of spectral pulse shaping introduced by
Colombeau C. Froehly et al in 1983 known as the zero dispersion line or 4f line
[2]. This simple and versatile method operates in the following way:
1. An ultrashort pulse is angularly dispersed into its spectral components via
diﬀraction grating
2. The angularly diﬀracted spectral components are focused via lens or mirror into
equally spaced diﬀraction spots in the Fourier plane.
12
==PAGE:23==
CHAPTER 2. PULSE SHAPING IN PRACTICE
3. The phase, intensity and/or polarization of each spatially separated spectral
component is manipulated in the Fourier plane via spatial light modulator.
4. After manipulation, the spatially distributed spectral components are focused
back down to a single point by another lens or mirror
5. The focused spectral elements are combined back into a temporal pulse distri-
bution by means of another diﬀraction grating
Femtosecond
pulse in
Shaped pulse
out
Mask
Figure 2.1: Schematic sketch of the 4f-line pulse shaper. The incoming unshaped pulse
is diﬀracted oﬀthe grating on the left side, and the spectral elements are focused by the
ﬁrst lens. After passing through the mask in the Fourier plane, the spectral components
are transformed back into a shaped spectral pulse by the second lens and grating.
A sketch of this device is shown in ﬁgure 2.1. It can be shown [2] that there is
a physically imposed limit to the frequency resolution in the Fourier plane. Due to
the nature of the Fourier transform, this corresponds to a limit on the size of the
time window that the shaped pulse may inhabit. The typical size of this window can
range from ∼24 [ps] to ∼78 [ps] for infrared [24] to mid-infrared [32] wavelengths,
respectively.
The spatial light modulator is also known as a mask, and the nature of the spectral
manipulations it performs will impact the shape of the recombined temporal pulse.
The shape of the temporal pulse is thereby decided by directly controlling the phase,
intensity and/or polarization of the spectral distribution using an adjustable mask.
13
==PAGE:24==
CHAPTER 2. PULSE SHAPING IN PRACTICE
The relationship between the spectral manipulations and the shape of the result-
ing temporal pulse is one of the primary topics of this thesis. For simplicity, only
alterations of the spectral phase will be considered.
2.2.1
The Spatial Light Modulator
The liquid crystal spatial light modulator (LC-SLM) is an example of an adjustable
spectral mask. This device independently modiﬁes the optical path of each spectral
component using an array of nematic liquid crystals that change their orientation
when a voltage is applied.
A representative sketch of such an array with typical
component sizes is shown in ﬁgure 2.2b, and ﬁgure 2.2b shows examples of two actual
devices. By assuming direct control over the modiﬁcation produced by each pixel,
it becomes possible to produce frequency dependent phase changes in the spectral
domain, the complexity of which is limited only by the pixel and frequency resolution
of the system.
x
SLM
100 µm
3 µm
9 µm
y
(a) Typical dimensions and geometry
of part of a LC-SLM pixel array. The
light gray areas contain the nematic liq-
uid crystals.

resolution LC-SLM (Meadowlark Optics)

==PAGE:25==
CHAPTER 2. PULSE SHAPING IN PRACTICE
2.2.2
Control Schemes
For simple molecules, where it is possible to make theoretical predictions, a so called
“open-loop” [2] scheme may be employed, where pulse shapes are designed and de-
ployed based on a priori knowledge of the quantum system (the last section in chapter
10 will take this approach). However, most molecular systems are far too complicated
for simple theoretical predictions to be of much help. In these cases, a so called “closed
loop” scheme may be deployed, in which a series of test pulses are applied to a system
and the impact on the system is measured in real time and fed into a computer that
is controlling the pulse shaper, as sketched in ﬁgure 2.3
Initial Pulse
Shaped Pulse
Flow 
Cell
Detector 
Grating 
LC-SLM 
Figure 2.3: Sketch of a closed-loop control system. A series of test pulses are applied to
the system, and the performance of each pulse conﬁguration is analyzed by measuring
the products of the reaction in the ﬂow cell. The results are fed into a computer which
updates the LC-SLM based on the performance using an optimization algorithm until
the best conﬁguration is found.
By analyzing the performance of the shaped pulses in this manner, it becomes
possible to iterate towards an optimal pulse shape using optimization software, e.g.
evolutionary algorithms. In 1991 this was demonstrated to be experimentally feasible
by R. S. Judson [16], and in the following years this method has found a number of
experimental applications, including manipulation of biological proteins [1], ionization
15
==PAGE:26==
CHAPTER 2. PULSE SHAPING IN PRACTICE
of gas phase diatomic sodium and state excitation in liquid phase methanol and
benzene [3], and the probing of chemical mechanisms via optimized pulse analysis [15].
Theoretical implementations of this type of setup making use of a genetic optimization
algorithm can be found in chapters 13 and 14 of this thesis.
16
==PAGE:27==
Chapter 3
Mathematical Description of
Ultrashort Pulses
3.1
Introduction
As explained in the previous chapter, the 4f-line pulse shaper operates by manipulat-
ing the pulse frequencies in the spectral domain. In this chapter, the mathematical
techniques for describing and characterizing ultrashort pulses in the frequency and
time domains will be introduced in order to facilitate a theoretical analysis of spectral
pulse shaping.
3.2
The Fourier Transform
In the semiclassical limit, a laser pulse can be described by an electric ﬁeld that varies
as a function of time:
ε(t)
Where ε(t) is a real function. It is often convenient to describe ε (t) as the real part
of a complex function E (t)
ε (t) = Re {E (t)}
17
==PAGE:28==
CHAPTER 3. MATHEMATICAL DESCRIPTION OF ULTRASHORT PULSES
E (t) can be represented in the spectral domain using the Fourier transform [6]. In
this thesis, the spectral distribution ˜E (ω) of complex temporal signal E (t) is given
by:
˜E (ω) =
Z ∞
−∞
E (t) exp (iωt) dt
Where ω denotes the angular frequency. The reverse Fourier transform of ˜E (ω) is
given by:
E(t) = 1
2π
Z ∞
−∞
˜E (ω) exp (−iωt) dt
This means that a laser pulse can be described either by its complex frequency distri-
bution ˜E (ω), the complex temporal function E(t) or the real temporal function ε (t).
The spectral distribution ˜E (ω) can be separated into real and complex parts:
˜E (ω) = A (ω) exp (iφ (ω))
Where A (ω) is the spectral amplitude and φ (ω) is the spectral phase.

some general properties of Gaussians and their Fourier transformations. Consider a

since this is the direction of propagation. Keeping this in mind, consider the repre-
sentative plot of the spacial distribution of a single cycle of the electric ﬁeld, frozen
at an instance in time, in ﬁgure 3.3.
At each location along the z axis, the electric ﬁeld is either pointing in the positive
or negative x direction, and over the course of a full cycle, the positive and negative
contributions to the ﬁeld cancel and the net ﬁeld in the x direction is 0. Intuitively,

When aω2
0 →∞, exp (−aω2
0) →0, and when aω2
0 →0, exp (−aω2
0) →1. Since
exp (−aω2
0) should, as per the above argument, in principle be 0, the smaller aω2
0
becomes, the more “unphysical” the light pulse becomes. This is illustrated in ﬁgures
3.4a and 3.4b, in which the pulse width is kept constant and the frequency is decreased
until exp (−aω2
0) becomes large.
In the left plot, the large number of oscillations contained by the envelope function
means that the positive and negative contributions to the transverse electric ﬁeld are
approximately equal and almost cancel out, and exp (−aω2
0) is very small. This indi-
cates that the mathematical description of the pulse in this limit is accurate. In the
right plot, it can be seen that the positive contribution to exp (−aω2
0) is signiﬁcantly
larger than the negative contribution, which means that the positive and negative
contributions do not cancel, resulting in a pulse that is unphysical. The spectral
distributions of the two pulses indicate that the more unphysical a pulse is, the more

negative frequencies are required to represent the pulse in Fourier space.
It is apparent that exp (−aω2
0) will never be absolutely 0 for any ﬁnite frequency
and pulse width. In practice, the implications of the argumentation in this section
will simply impose a lower limit on the central pulse frequency with respect to the
width of the pulse envelope. For all applications in this thesis where pulses of various
widths and frequencies are investigated, this limit will be set to exp (−aω2
0) < 0.05.
3.5
Pulse Energy
The intensity of an electromagnetic wave is deﬁned as the rate of energy transport per
unit area, and is typically given in units of [TW/cm2]. This quantity is described by
the Poynting vector in equation 3.7. As stated in the previous section, the magnetic
ﬁeld of an electromagnetic wave is perpendicular to the electric ﬁeld, and Maxwells
equations show that the relationship between the electric ﬁeld amplitude E = |E|
and magnetic ﬁeld amplitude B = |B| is given by B = E/c [10]. This means that the
magnitude of the electromagnetic ﬁeld intensity I can be expressed in terms of the
electric ﬁeld amplitude:
I =
1
cµ0
|E|2
(3.8)
The energy of an ultrashort pulse pulse is given by the intensity integrated over time.
In this thesis the energy unit will be given in units of [J/cm2] . Consider the temporal
electric ﬁeld of a typical Gaussian laser pulse with peak amplitude A0:
ε (t) = A0 cos (ωt) exp
 −αt2
The intensity as a function of time will be given by:
I (t) = A2

Chapter 4
Analytic Spectral Chirps
4.1
Introduction
In section 3.2 of the previous chapter, it was stated that the spectral distribution of
a pulse ˜E (ω) can be divided into real and imaginary parts:
˜E (ω) = A (ω) exp (iφ (ω))
Where A (ω) is the spectral amplitude and φ (ω) is the spectral phase. As stated in
chapter 2, the 4f-line pulse shaper can manipulate the shape of the temporal pulse
by using the LC-SLM to change the spectral phase.
The following sections of this chapter will go over some of the general analyti-
cal results that can be derived with respect to spectral phase shifting, along with
introducing the Wigner transform, which can be used to help characterize complex
pulses.
4.2
The Spectral Phase Chirp
The transform of Fourier limited Gaussian pulse will be a spectral distribution with
a ﬂat phase [6]. A ﬂat phase indicates that all of the oscillating components of the
28
==PAGE:39==
CHAPTER 4. ANALYTIC SPECTRAL CHIRPS
spectral distribution have the same phase angle at t = 0. Dividing the spectral dis-
tribution into a number of discrete components with frequency dependent amplitude
An helps illustrate this concept:
E (t) = A1 cos (ω1t) + A2 cos (ω2t) + . . . + An cos (ωnt)
A phase shift in the spectral domain means that a phase angle is added to one or
more of these oscillators:
E (t) = A1 cos (ω1t + φ1) + A2 cos (ω2t + φ2) + . . . + An cos (ωnt + φn)
This change in phase in the spectral domain will result in a change in the temporal
pulse shape when the spectral distribution is transformed back into the temporal
domain. In this thesis, changing the phase of a spectral distribution will henceforth
be referred to as a spectral chirp. This is not to be confused with a temporal pulse
frequency that varies over time, which will henceforth be referred to as a temporal
chirp. Parsevals theorem [6, 17] states that the relationship between a function x (t)
and its Fourier transform X (ω) is unitary, i.e.:
Z ∞
−∞
|x (t)|2 dt = 1
2π
Z ∞
−∞
|X (ω)|2 dω
This leads to the important result that a spectrally chirped pulse will always have
the same energy as the unchirped pulse. In the following section, a few of the ana-
lytical results that can be obtained for speciﬁc spectral phase chirp functions will be
discussed.
29
==PAGE:40==
CHAPTER 4. ANALYTIC SPECTRAL CHIRPS

4.3.1

The simplest case to consider is what happens to the temporal pulse if all frequencies

the envelope function remains unchanged. If the pulse envelope is very narrow and
only contains a few cycles, changing the phase in this manner can have a signiﬁcant
impact on the overall pulse shape [2], however for many-cycle pulses this temporal
phase change will be less signiﬁcant.

4.4
The Wigner Distribution
The previous sections in this chapter have shown how a series of relatively simple pulse
shapes can be represented in both temporal and spectral domains. The concept of
instantaneous frequency has been introduced, and it has been shown that some types
of spectral chirp can change the envelope shape and/or produce chirped temporal
frequencies in a pulse. Many of the pulses that will be considered in the subsequent
chapters of this thesis are too complex to be evaluated analytically. The features of
shaped pulses of this nature can be challenging to interpret. The Wigner distribution,
which has origins in quantum mechanics, has proven to be a useful tool for visualizing
ultrashort pulses as a function of both frequency and time [28]. The general expression
for the Wigner distribution of spectral pulse distribution ˜E (ω) is given by:
W (ω, t) = 1

Chapter 5
Numerical Analysis of Higher Order
Chirps
5.1
Introduction
The last chapter presented analytical solutions to the reverse Fourier transformation,
and Wigner distributions, of a number of simple spectral chirp functions. In practice,
spectral chirp transformations are often very complex and will therefore require nu-
meric solutions. The ﬁrst part of this chapter will discuss the implementation of the
discrete Fourier transform for this purpose. In the second part, the discrete Fourier
transform will be used to analyze a few higher order spectral chirp functions.
5.2
The Discrete Fourier Transform
The data of any signal, analytic or otherwise, will be represented on a computer as a
series of discrete points. While the previous sections have dealt with analytic Fourier
transforms, the transform of a discrete series of signal data points will be performed
using the discrete Fourier transform (DFT) [29].
As has been stated earlier, the

There are a few important diﬀerences to take note of when using the DFT instead
of the analytical Fourier transform. As stated, the spectral resolution ∆ω is given by
∆ω = 2π
T
This means that if the goal is to numerically calculate the shape of a spectrally
chirped pulse, it is important to choose a large enough T to allow for a good fre-
quency resolution. If the resolution is too low, it will limit the number of available
independent spectral frequencies that a spectral chirp will be able to inﬂuence. Figure
5.1 illustrates this point.

sampling frequencies. The full length of the temporal signal on the left side is T = 10,
and on the right side T = 40. This is reﬂected in the spectral distributions, in which
it can be seen that the number of discrete frequencies representing the signal increases
when T is larger by a factor of 4. The impact of an insuﬃcient spectral resolution on
the numeric calculation of the shaped pulse is illustrated in ﬁgure 5.2.
The left ﬁgure shows the result of applying a quadratic spectral chirp to the T = 10
pulse, compared to the analytic solution, and the right ﬁgure shows the T = 40 pulse
with an identical chirp, also compared to the analytic solution. Here it is clear to see
that the discrepancy between the numeric and analytic solutions is larger for decreased
spectral resolutions. In general, the more complex the spectral chirp function is, the
greater the spectral resolution will need to be for the numeric calculation to be close
to the analytic solution. It should be noted that, as described in chapter 2, in an
experimental situation pulse shapers operate by splitting the pulse frequencies into
discrete bins of ﬁnite spectral width. This means that for all practical purposes, the
ﬁnite spectral resolution of the DFT complements the limit on the spectral resolution

The last remark to make about the DFT is that the sampling frequency of the
temporal signal must be at least twice as large as the highest frequency contained
within the signal, as per the Nyqvist–Shannon sampling theorem [29]. Of course, a
Gaussian pulse will in principle contain all frequencies, but due to the exponential
decay of the tail it can be considered bandwidth-limited within a certain intensity
threshold.
5.3
The Quartic/Quintic Chirp
Having demonstrated the methodology behind the DFT, it will now be used to ﬁnd
the temporal shapes of pulses with spectral chirps that are of higher than cubic orders,
for which analytic expressions become too cumbersome. Figures 5.3 and 5.4 show the
spectral chirp function, shaped pulse, instantaneous frequency, and numerical Wigner
distribution resulting from spectral chirps of quartic and quintic orders, respectively.
48
==PAGE:59==

The instantaneous frequencies in the second panels are calculated numerically by
ﬁnding the zero crossings of the oscillating part of the pulse, where the time interval
between each zero crossing is interpreted as half an oscillation period. For comparison,
the initial unshaped pulse here is identical to the 10 [fs] FWHM, 800 [nm] wavelength
pulse that was analyzed in chapter 4.

The quarticly chirped pulse shape in 5.3 is reminiscent of the quadratically chirped
pulse. The slope of the instantaneous frequency near the center of the pulse is ap-
proximately linear, as in the quadratic case. However, the outer edges of the quarticly
chirped pulse have an extra component, which is reﬂected in the nonlinearity of the in-
stantaneous frequency distribution. This can also be seen in the Wigner distribution,
50
==PAGE:61==
CHAPTER 5. NUMERICAL ANALYSIS OF HIGHER ORDER CHIRPS
which indicates a nonlinear shift in frequency as a function of time.
The shape, instantaneous frequency distribution and Wigner distribution of the
quinticly chirped pulse in ﬁgure 5.4 is also reminiscent of the cubicly chirped pulse.
It is interesting to note that the temporal pulse shape and instantaneous frequency
distributions for both the quadratic and quartic chirped pulses appear to be symmet-
rical around t = 0. Additionally, the cubic and quintic chirped pulses both seem to
share a lack of frequency shift, and asymmetric pulse envelopes. It will be argued in
chapter 6 that all of these similarities are a result of the symmetry properties of the
spectral chirp functions.

Figure 5.6: Variations in the second moment pulse width ∆t for various combinations
of chirp orders, from quadratic to quintic.
Another consideration is how the pulse widths are inﬂuenced by combined chirp
orders. Figure 5.6 shows how the second moment pulse widths vary for a series of
chirp order combinations from quadratic to quintic order
As the ﬁgure shows, for most higher order chirp combinations, the pulse width is
at its widest when chirp orders are combined, i.e. combining a quadratic chirp with a
cubic chirp will result in a pulse that is wider than the pulse shaped by the same cubic
or quadratic chirp alone. There are two exceptions to this rule however; combining
quadratic and quartic, or cubic and quintic coeﬃcients, of opposite sign will result
53
==PAGE:64==
CHAPTER 5. NUMERICAL ANALYSIS OF HIGHER ORDER CHIRPS
in a “cancelation” of the pulse width increase. This is apparent in the asymmetries
present in the top right and bottom left plots of ﬁgure 5.6.

The shape and instantaneous frequencies of the pulses resulting from these two
types of combined chirps have been plotted in ﬁgures 5.7 and 5.8. The temporal

oscillate. The cubic/quintic chirp combinations, while inﬂuencing the pulse shapes,
do not appear to produce temporal frequency chirp.

Chapter 6
The Quadratic Chirp & Chirp
Symmetries
6.1
Introduction
In the past two chapters, the transformed temporal shapes of a number of diﬀerent
spectral chirp-types have been demonstrated via analytic and numerical methods.
This chapter will attempt to derive some generalities pertaining to the results that
have been covered so far. The ﬁrst part is an in-depth analysis of the quadratically
chirped pulse, which is a good model to investigate due to its dynamics and relative
simplicity. The second part of this chapter contains a series of arguments based on
the symmetry properties of spectral chirp functions.
6.2
Analyzing the Quadratic Chirp
As the preceding sections have shown, as the complexity of the spectral chirp in-
creases, the complexity of the shaped temporal pulse quickly increases as well. Due to
the highly nonlinear relationship between spectral chirps and temporal pulse shapes,
it can be diﬃcult derive general rules about their relationships.
56
==PAGE:67==
CHAPTER 6. THE QUADRATIC CHIRP & CHIRP SYMMETRIES
That being said, the quadratic chirp is an excellent candidate for closer study,
as it is simple enough to allow for the derivation of analytical results, as well being
complex enough to possess important features such as alterations to the temporal
pulse shape and instantaneous frequency.
In section 3, the concept of the second moment pulse width, given by equation
3.3, was introduced.

6.3
Analyzing Chirp Symmetries
Having examined a number of analytic and numeric examples of spectrally chirped
pulses, there are a few features that are worth investigating in greater detail:
• Spectral chirps that are odd functions around the central pulse frequency ω0
produce asymmetrical temporal pulse envelope functions.
60
==PAGE:71==
CHAPTER 6. THE QUADRATIC CHIRP & CHIRP SYMMETRIES
• Spectral chirps that are odd functions around the central pulse frequency ω0 do
not induce a change in the instantaneous temporal frequency over time
• Spectral chirps that are even functions around the central pulse frequency ω0
result in symmetric temporal envelope functions.
• Spectral chirps that are even functions around the central pulse frequency ω0
will induce a change in the instantaneous temporal frequency over time. The
temporal instantaneous frequency chirps appear as odd functions that are an-
tisymmetric around centroid of the temporal pulse.
In the following subsections, mathematical arguments will be made to support the
veracity of these postulations.
6.3.1
Odd Spectral Chirps Envelope and Frequencies
Assume the reverse Fourier transform of a pulse centered at ω = ω0 with generalized
real, odd chirp function godd (ω −ω0):
E (t) = 1
2π
Z ∞
−∞
exp
 −(ω −ω0)2
exp (igodd (ω −ω0)) exp (−iωt) dω
It can be shown (appendix D.1) that E (t)will have the general form:
E (t) = 1
2π exp (−iω0t) Fasym(t)
Where Fasym(t) is a real, asymmetrical function. The upshot of this is that using an
odd function as the chirp function will result in asymmetric modiﬁcations to the pulse
envelope, but will not be able to induce frequency shifts in the underlying complex
frequency components.
61
==PAGE:72==
CHAPTER 6. THE QUADRATIC CHIRP & CHIRP SYMMETRIES
6.3.2
Even Spectral Chirps Envelope
Now, assume the Fourier transform of a pulse centered at ω = ω0 with generalized
real, even chirp function geven (ω −ω0)
E (t) = 1
2π
Z ∞
−∞
exp
 −(ω −ω0)2
exp (igeven (ω −ω0)) exp (−iωt) dω
Using a similar level of argumentation as in the previous case with the odd chirp func-
tion, it can be shown (see Appendix D.2) that for even chirps, the reverse transformed
temporal function must obey the following relation:
|E (−t)| = |E (t)|
Since |E (t)| is the deﬁnition of the envelope function, the above relation shows that
for symmetric frequency chirps, the envelope function will always be a symmetric,
even function centered around t = 0.
6.3.3
Even Chirps, Frequency Shifting
Now, an argument will be made with respect to the last claim, which is that an even
spectral chirp will result in an odd temporal instantaneous frequency function. Before
continuing, a simpliﬁcation will be made. Whereas until now the Gaussian spectral
distribution has been interpreted as a continuous symmetrical function around ω0, to
help facilitate the following argument, this continuous distribution will be replaced
by a simpler model: a spectral decomposition that only consists of three discrete
oscillating components:
E (t) = exp (i (ω0 −∆ω) t) + exp (iω0t) + exp (i (ω0 + ∆ω) t)
(6.3)
62
==PAGE:73==
CHAPTER 6. THE QUADRATIC CHIRP & CHIRP SYMMETRIES
This will function as a model system the behavior of which can be generalized to the
case of N oscillators. Applying an even chirp to the spectral distribution in equation
6.3 can be interpreted as adding a phase θ to the ﬁrst and third components:
E (t) = exp (i (ω0 −∆ω) t + iθ) + exp (iω0t) + exp (i (ω0 + ∆ω) t + iθ)
(of course, adding θ to the central component would also be considered an even chirp,
but would correspond to trivial constant chirp function described in section 4.3.1,
which does not lead to a temporal frequency shift). Now, consider the case where t
assumes an arbitrary positive value t = ∆t, ∆t > 0. It can be shown (see Appendix
D.3 ) that
Re {E (∆t)} = [2 cos (∆ω∆t) cos (ω0∆t + θ) + cos (ω0∆t)]
(6.4)
likewise, it can be shown (see Appendix D.3 ) that for cases where t = −∆t
Re {E (−∆t)} = [2 cos (∆ω∆t) cos (ω0∆t −θ) + cos (ω0∆t)]
(6.5)
The interpretation of these results can be facilitated by assuming 2 cos (∆ω∆t) is
a slowly varying envelope function. Inspecting the above equations, it can be seen
that when 2 cos (∆ω∆t) = 0, then Re {E (∆t)} = Re {E (−∆t)}. However, when
cos (∆ω∆t) becomes larger as ∆t changes, it “turns up” the phase-shifted components
cos (ω0∆t + θ) and cos (ω0∆t −θ) for positive and negative times, respectively. The
result is that as ∆t moves in the positive and negative directions away from t = 0,
the instantaneous frequency will be shifted away from its initial value in equal and
opposite directions.
This eﬀect is illustrated in ﬁgure 6.3 where E (t) for negative times has been
mirrored across the y axis.
The ﬁgure shows how the locations of the red peaks
63
==PAGE:74==
CHAPTER 6. THE QUADRATIC CHIRP & CHIRP SYMMETRIES
(t < 0) and the blue peaks (t > 0) begin to shift away from the locations of the
unchirped black peaks in equal and opposite directions as time goes by. This results
in an odd instantaneous frequency distribution centered around t = 0.

Figure 6.3: Equations 6.5 and 6.4 show how the oscillating frequency decreases for
positive times, and increases for negative times by an equal amount. Note how the
positions of the blue peaks slowly shift forward until they match the position of the non-
phase shifted peak (black), indicating a stretching of the wavelength, i.e. a decrease in
frequency. Conversely, note how the positions of the red peaks slowly shift backwards
until they match the non-phase shifted peak, indicating a wavelength shortening, i.e.
an increase in frequency. The parameters here are ∆ω = 0.1, ω0 = 0.5, and θ = π/2.

Part III
Time-Dependent Quantum Mechanics
65
==PAGE:76==
Chapter 7
Ehrenfest’s Theorem
7.1
Introduction
There will now be a departure from the topic of spectral pulse shaping. The next
few chapters will deal with mathematical methods of describing the semiclassical
interaction of a time-dependent electric ﬁeld with a quantum system. Following the
spirit of part II of this thesis, this ﬁrst chapter on the topic will deal with some of
the analytic results that can be obtained.
7.2
The Time-Dependent Schrödinger Equation
Many quantum mechanics textbooks start by introducing the time dependent Schrödinger
equation (TDSE):
iℏ∂
∂tΨ (x, t) = ˆHΨ (x, t)
(7.1)
Where
ˆH = −ℏ2
2m

(for simplicity, all examples covered in this chapter will be one dimensional) It is
then often shown how a separation of variables can be achieved by letting Ψ (x, t) be

written as

This leads to two diﬀerential equations:
iℏ˙χ (t) = Eχ (t)
(7.2)

Equation 7.2 is easily solved:
χ (t) = χ0 exp



Equation 7.3 is the well-known time independent Schrödinger equation (TISE). The
solutions to the TISE are the stationary eigenfunctions and eigenenergies associated
with the system, and much attention is focused on their derivation. It often feels
almost like an afterthought when the time-dependent part of Ψ (x, t) is added to ﬁnd
the full solution:
Ψ (x, t) = ψE (x) χ0 exp


ℏEt

(7.4)
(In practice, χ0 is absorbed into the normalization factor). The probability distribu-
tion is given by:
|Ψ (x, t)|2
The squared norm means that the time dependent part of Ψ (x, t) drops out, and
therefore, the probability distribution does not change as a function of time. It is
important, however, to keep in mind that the form in equation 7.4 is only a particular
solution. In nature, most quantum systems evolve and change over time, and this
dynamic behavior is a result of the fact that solutions to the TDSE can be expressed
as linear combinations of solutions of the form given by equation 7.4. For discrete
spectra:

The superpositions of the complex phases lead to the probability distribution changing
over time. A localized wave function probability distribution that moves as a unit in
time is known as a wave packet.

|Ψ (x, t)|2 = 1

is normalized at t = 0, will remain normalized for all subsequent times as well.

7.4
Ehrenfest’s Theorem
The fact that the expectation position and velocity of the free particle wave packet
obey classical equations of motion is a speciﬁc example of Erhenfest’s theorem, which
states that the expectation values of a wave packet moving in a potential that is
of quadratic order or less will behave in a classical manner. Speciﬁcally, Erhenfests
theorem states that the relationship between the expectation values of the position q
and the momentum p, of a wave packet can be expressed by the following diﬀerential
equations:
d
dt ⟨p⟩= ⟨p⟩
m
(7.8)
d
dt ⟨p⟩=

−∂V
∂q

(7.9)
Unfortunately, the above expressions cannot be considered a closed system, as in
order to calculate
D
−∂V
∂q
E
for a given time, the form of the wave function is needed.
If the expectation values of the wave packet behaved classically, the above equations
could be easily solved, as they would have the form:
d
dt ⟨q⟩= ⟨p⟩
m
(7.10)
d
dt ⟨p⟩= −∂V (⟨q⟩)
∂⟨q⟩
(7.11)
It can be shown that equations 7.8 and 7.9 simplify to the classical form given by
expressions 7.10 and 7.11 when the order of the potential V (q) is quadratic or less (for
a full derivation of Erhenfests theorem and the special case, see Appendix E.3). This
important result allows for some intuition to be gained about the dynamic temporal
evolution of a quantum wave packet.

7.5
Gaussian Wave Packets
Consider a trial solution to the TDSE in the form of a generalized Gaussian function:
Ψ(x) = N exp


(7.12)
Where αt, pt, xt and γt are (possibly) time dependent functions and N is a real
normalization factor. Inserting this trial function into the TDSE, for potentials that
are quadratic or less, leads to a simple yet powerful technique of calculating time
dependent analytic solutions by expressing αt, pt, xt and γt as a series of coupled
diﬀerential equations . This method is discussed in considerable detail in appendices
F.1, F.2 and F.3 W.R.T. the free particle, and linear/harmonic/parabolic potentials.
However, for the purpose of this thesis the most important result is that of the forced
harmonic potential:

==PAGE:84==
CHAPTER 7. EHRENFEST’S THEOREM
As will be shown in later chapters, it is important to consider the amplitude term
A0 in equations 7.14 and 7.15. Take note that the amplitude of the wave packet
oscillations (and therefore the system energy) in the non-resonant case can still be
driven to extremes if A0 is very large. This indicates that there are conceptually two
diﬀerent ways to drive a system into higher energy levels; one in which the wave packet
“climbs the rungs” of the energy ladder, by being driven by a resonant frequency, and
one in which the wave packet gets “kicked” into higher energy states by virtue of a
large driving amplitude.

==PAGE:85==
Chapter 8
Quantum Numerical Methods
8.1
Introduction
In the previous chapter, a few analytical solutions to the TDSE were derived and
discussed. While these types of analyses can facilitate the physical understanding
and intuition of how quantum mechanical systems evolve over time, in practice most
systems are too complex to allow for analytical solutions. This chapter will describe
the methodology behind obtaining numerical solutions to the TDSE and the TISE.
8.2

Hilbert space representation. For a calculation scheme, the Hilbert space must be
discrete. The construction of such a Hilbert space can be achieved using the collo-
cation method [18]. consider an arbitrary function f (x), approximated by a linear
combination of another set of functions:

The Fourier Grid Hamiltonian Method
Having introduced a numerical method of time-propagation, there will now be a brief
discussion of the numerical technique used to solve the TISE. The Fourier grid Hamil-

Hamiltonian of a discrete system can be constructed using an N × N matrix of the

8.5
A Test of the Quantum Numerical Methods
Having introduced the numerical techniques that will be employed to solve the TDSE
and TISE, this section will demonstrate their performance on a few simple systems.
First, the SO method was used to examine the time evolution of a Gaussian wave
packet interacting with a parabolic potential (the analytic solution to this system is
derived in appendix F.3) The simulation results at four snapshots in time are shown
in ﬁgure 8.2b , and the analytic solutions at the same intervals are shown in ﬁgure
8.2a
Here it can be seen that the numeric and analytic solutions follow each other closely
until the edges of the wave packet reach the boundaries of the simulation space in
panel 4. Here, the numerical wave packet exhibits high frequency oscillations which
are not in accordance with the analytical solution. The problem can be tracked to the
periodic boundary conditions of the system, which will lead to unphysical behavior
81
==PAGE:92==
CHAPTER 8. QUANTUM NUMERICAL METHODS
if care is not taken to ensure that the wave packet remains well localized within
the simulation space. This issue can be mitigated by employing imaginary “gobbler”
potentials at the simulation boundaries which eﬀectively absorb the wave function.
In this thesis, these imaginary potentials have been employed in the Morse potential
model, and are discussed in detail appendix H.2.
A ﬁnal test was performed in which the SO method was used to propagate an
initially Gaussian wave packet in two diﬀerent potentials of quartic order. Here, for
the ﬁrst time, is an example of a system where Erhenfests theorem breaks down, i.e.
the expectation values will no longer behave classically due to the potential being
of higher than quadratic order. A time series plot of the wave packet moving in a
potential with the form of an asymmetric double well is shown in ﬁgure 8.3a. Here
it is observed that the wave packet does not remain Gaussian as time progresses.
Figure 8.3b shows the time series evolution of a Gaussian wave packet placed in the
center of a symmetrical double well potential, here it can again be seen that the initial
Gaussian form is quickly lost as time progresses. In addition to this, the shape of
the wave packet remains symmetrical throughout the simulation due to the overall
symmetry of the system.
In addition to propagating the wave function in time, numeric solutions to the
stationary eigenstates of the two quartic potentials were calculated using the FGH
method. The wave packets were then projected onto these eigenfunctions in order to
examine the distribution of states. The eigenfunctions and state occupancy of the
asymmetric potential simulation are shown in ﬁgures 8.4a and 8.4b.
In ﬁgure 8.4b it can be seen that the wave packet is mostly occupying states 5−10,
with the highest occupation level at state 8. This is indicative of an elevated system
energy due to the initial wave packet being placed some distance from the ground
state.
The eigenfunctions and projected states of the symmetric potential are shown in
ﬁgures 8.4c and 8.4d. The occupied state levels indicate that the wave packet is only

Chapter 9
The Born-Oppenheimer

9.1
Introduction
It is now time to discuss how the numerical techniques for solving the TDSE and TISE
can be applied to real physical systems. This chapter deals with the background of
approaches that allow for relatively simple mathematical model approximations.
9.2
The Born-Oppenheimer Approximation
An arbitrary molecular system is composed of electrons and nuclei with opposite
charges. In principle, to fully describe the molecular behavior it is necessary to solve
the TDSE with a Hamiltonian that includes the kinetic energy of all K electrons and
all N nuclei:
ˆTelec =
K
X
i=1
ˆp2
i
2me
ˆTnuc =
N
X
g=1
ˆP 2
g
2Mg
85
==PAGE:96==
CHAPTER 9. THE BORN-OPPENHEIMER APPROXIMATION
Additionally, the Hamiltonian must include the electron-electron and nuclear-nuclear
repulsion terms:
ˆVelec =
K
X
i=1
K
X
j>i
e2
4πϵ0rij
ˆVnuc =
N
X
g=1
N
X
h>g
ZgZhe2
4πϵ0rgh
(Zi is the atomic number of the ith nucleus) And a term the describes the electron-
nuclear attraction:
ˆVint = −
K
X
i=1
N
X
g=1
Zge2
4πϵ0rig
The full Hamiltonian for a molecular system then becomes
ˆHmol = ˆTelec + ˆTnuc + ˆVelec + ˆVnuc + ˆVint
And the full TDSE is therefore:
iℏ∂Ψ (r, R, t)
∂t
= ˆHmolΨ (r, R, t)
Where the R coordinates indicate the locations of the nuclei and the r coordinates are
the locations of the electrons. This equation can be very diﬃcult to solve. A useful
approximation can be made by exploiting the fact that the nuclei are approximately
three orders of magnitude heavier than the electrons. This means that most often,
the nuclei are moving much slower than the electrons. It can therefore be assumed
that at any given time, from the electrons point of view, the nuclei are stationary at
ﬁxed positions R. This greatly simpliﬁes the Hamiltonian of the system. For a ﬁxed
set of nuclear coordinates at R, The corresponding electronic wave functions ψn and
energies can be found by solving a TISE of the form:
ˆHelec(r; R)ψ(r; R) = E (R) ψ(r; R)
86
==PAGE:97==
CHAPTER 9. THE BORN-OPPENHEIMER APPROXIMATION
Where Helec = Telec + Velec + Vnuc + Vint. This is known as the adiabatic approxi-
mation. This means that for every possible spacial conﬁguration of nuclei, a set of
corresponding stationary electronic wave functions ψn can be calculated with corre-
sponding energies En. Within the adiabatic approximation, the wave function of the
full system can therefore be expressed as
Ψ (r, R, t) = χ (R, t) ψn (r; R)
Where χ (R, t) is the wave function that describes the nuclear motion and ψn (r; R)
is the electronic wave function that corresponds to the nuclear locations R. Plug-
ging this expression into the TDSE arrives at the equation that describes the time
dependent behavior of the molecular system within the adiabatic approximation:

This is what is known as the Born-Oppenheimer (BO) approximation, and is an
important and useful tool for solving a large variety of molecular systems. En (R)
eﬀectively acts as a potential landscape which directs the motion of the nuclear wave
function, in the same general sense as that of a particle moving in a potential, which
has already been discussed in previous chapters. For larger molecules, this potential
landscape can be very complex, however this thesis will only deal with simple diatomic
systems where the interatomic distance is the only coordinate variable. The potential
landscape of such a system is often described using a parameterized function such as
the Morse model, which will be introduced in chapter 10.

CHAPTER 9. THE BORN-OPPENHEIMER APPROXIMATION

description. Consider the Lagrangian of a system consisting of two particles with

only depends on the separation distance |rB −rA|:

9.3
Simulating a Molecule
There will now be a brief description of how the theories outlined in the ﬁrst sections
of this chapter can be used to model the interaction of time dependent electric ﬁelds
with simple diatomic molecular systems in the ground state.
In this thesis, this
interaction is described semi-classically, i.e. as quantum molecules in classical electric
ﬁelds, since it has been shown [26] that for intense electric ﬁelds, this approximation
will hold.
As stated in subsection 9.2.1, rotation will not be taken into account,
i.e.
the dynamics of the system can be described using one coordinate variable.
Additionally, the assumption will be made that the time dependent electric ﬁeld is
always parallel to R, i.e. θ = 0 in equation 9.1. This allows for a (relatively) simple
way to solve the interaction between an electric ﬁeld and a molecular ground state
using the Hamiltonian
ˆH = ˆH1 + ˆC11 = ˆTnuc + E1 (R) + ˆC11 (R)
Where Tnuc is the nuclear kinetic energy, E1 (R) is the ground state electronic po-
tential energy landscape calculated using the BO approximation and ˆC11 (R) is the
coupling strength between the electric ﬁeld ε (t) and the molecule as a function of R,
as described in section 9.2.2:
ˆC11 = −µ11(R) · ε (t)
Using the ﬁrst order expansion of µ(R) around Re :
µ11(R) ≈µ11 (Re) + [R −Re] µ′

expression now becomes:
ˆH = ˆTnuc + E1 (R) −Rµ′

To numerically simulate the time evolution of this simple system, it is necessary to

the center of mass, ˆTnuc can be solved using the methods outlined in section 8.2.
ˆV (R) describes the eﬀective potential that the nuclei are moving in at any given time
step, which consists of both the electronic contribution and the contribution from the
external ﬁeld. Using the same formalism as in section 8.2, this can be expressed in
matrix form for N spacial grid components:

Chapter 10
The OH Morse Potential
10.1
Introduction
Having shown how the interaction between an electric ﬁeld and a simple molecular
system can be described mathematically within the BO approximation, it is time to
introduce one of the primary models that will be examined in this thesis; the diatomic
OH system, the parameters of which represent the local OH bonds in the H2O and
HOD molecules. This model was chosen in part because it is common, appearing
in water, and in part because it has been the subject of numerous other theoretical
investigations [39, 26, 38].
10.2
The OH Molecule
The simplest possible model of a diatomic molecule is probably the harmonic oscilla-
tor, which was discussed in chapter 7. However, this model has certain important lim-
itations; it cannot describe unbound states, and it does not display the anharmonicity
of a real chemical bond. The Morse potential is able to capture these dynamics, and
is a relatively simple parameterized mathematical function of the following form [26]:
95
==PAGE:106==
CHAPTER 10. THE OH MORSE POTENTIAL
interatomic distance [a0]
0

OH Morse Potential Function
De
re
Figure 10.1: Sketch of the OH Morse potential. re denotes the equilibrium position
and De is the well depth. The ﬁgure shows how the energy goes towards 0 when the
interatomic distance becomes very large, which captures the dynamics of dissociation.
VMorse (R) = De [exp (−2a (R −re)) −2 exp (−a (R −re))]
Where De denotes the depth of the potential well (i.e. the dissociation energy), re
denotes the equilibrium bond distance, and a is a parameter that inﬂuences the well
width. A plot of the Morse potential function with parameters ﬁtted to the OH
molecule is shown in ﬁgure 10.1:
As the ﬁgure shows, the energy goes to 0 as the interatomic distance increases. In
this way a model of dissociation can be achieved, as when the two atoms are moved
inﬁnitely far from each other, they cease to feel any mutual attraction. In addition to
being able to model some of the important basic behavior of a real molecular system,
the Morse potential is also one of the few systems for which analytical eigenstate solu-
tions exist [8]. The Morse potential function can be expanded around the equilibrium
point in the form of a harmonic potential:
Vharm (R) = De

, and the reduced atomic mass Mred = 1728.539 [me]. [26]
This approximation will hold as long as the interatomic distance does not move too far
from re. The anharmonicity of the Morse oscillator can be visualized by comparing the
eigenenergies of the harmonic expansion to the eigenenergies of the Morse potential,
as shown in 10.2:
It is a well known fact that the energy diﬀerence between two adjacent eigenstates
in the quantum harmonic oscillator is always ℏω. The anharmonic Morse potential
however, does not follow this rule, and the size of the energy spacings decreases as the
energy level increases until the dissociation threshold is reached, after which bound-
state eigenfunctions no longer exist. The change in the eigenenergy spacing for the
Morse potential is given by the following analytical expression [8]:
E(n + 1) −E(n) = hν0 −(n + 1) (hν0)2
2De
(10.1)
97
==PAGE:108==
CHAPTER 10. THE OH MORSE POTENTIAL
Where h is Planck’s constant and
ν0 = a
2π
r
2De
Mred
Where Mred is the reduced mass of the system. E(n+1)−E(n) can be interpreted as
the discrete diﬀerential of the energy as a function of the state level n. Considering
10.1, it can be seen that the change in energy spacing for the Morse potential is linear
as a function of n. This important result means that in order to induce resonant
transitions in the Morse oscillator, the frequency of the driving ﬁeld should ideally
change over time. This will be illustrated in the subsequent sections of this chapter
by way of numerical simulations.
10.3
Numerical Parameters
Before performing any numerical simulations, care must be taken when selecting the
parameters of the simulation in order to:
1. Ensure that the desired level of accuracy is achieved
2. Ensure that the simulation can be performed within a reasonable timeframe
Parameters such as time step size, spacial grid step size, and the location of the
simulation space boundaries are important to consider. Assuming the correct model
is chosen, it may be possible to achieve an arbitrary level of accuracy by simply
increasing the resolution of the simulation parameters. However, this may drasti-
cally increase the computational workload. In practice it is therefore best to select
parameters that balance accuracy and computational eﬃciency.
For systems without analytical solutions, determining the absolute accuracy of the
numeric results may be impossible and often convergence methods are used. However,
the forced harmonic oscillator from chapter 7 is a simple time dependent quantum
system for which analytical solutions have been derived.
98
==PAGE:109==
CHAPTER 10. THE OH MORSE POTENTIAL
10.3.1
Time Step Size
The fact that the harmonic oscillator and Morse potential match up at low energies
indicates that it can be informative to consider the harmonic oscillator as a reference
system for the performance of the numerical methods that will be employed when
solving the Morse system. Given a time dependent electric ﬁeld ε (t), the Hamiltonian
of the harmonic expansion around the Morse potential equilibrium distance can be
expressed as:
ˆH = −
ℏ2
2Mred
∂2
∂R2 + De

a2 (R −re)2 −1

+ Rµ′
11ε (t)
(10.2)
Where µ′
11 is the slope of the OH Mecke function (equation 9.3) evaluated at re.
Equation 10.2 is reminiscent of the form of the forced harmonic potential seen in
equation 7.13 from chapter 7. As the analytic solutions from chapter 7 have shown,
driving the ground state with a resonant frequency will cause the amplitude of the
wave packet oscillations to increase linearly with time, exciting the system into higher
states as the wave packet climbs the energy rungs of the system.
By comparing analytic and numeric results from the time propagation of a reso-
nantly forced harmonic potential, parameterized as a second order expansion around
the equilibrium distance of the OH Morse potential, optimal simulation parameters
for the Morse potential were estimated. (The details of this analysis can be found in
appendix H.1)
The convergence data shows that computation times of only a few fractions of
a second yield error percentages of well under 1% when simulating the system for
600 [fs].
A step size of 0.06 [fs] was chosen as the optimum, yielding an error of
∼0.07% and a computation time of ∼0.35 seconds for 10000 propagation steps.
99
==PAGE:110==
CHAPTER 10. THE OH MORSE POTENTIAL
10.3.2
Grid Spacing
The FFT algorithm performs best when the number of components is equal to 2N.
It can also be shown [35] that the relationship between the maximum energy of the
system is inversely proportional the spatial grid step size, i.e. the higher the energy
of the system, the smaller the grid spacing steps must be. To this end, the optimal
grid spacing was selected by performing the aforementioned time step analysis with
an increasing number of grid points that were = 2N, until the simulation results
converged i.e. ceased to change when the grid resolution was further increased. This
optimal value was found to be 28 = 256 grid points, distributed over a domain with
total length L = 8 [a0], leading to an optimal grid step size of ∆x = 0.0312 [a0]
10.3.3
Domain Size
The simulation domain of the Morse potential was chosen to run from R = 0−16 [a0],
over 512 evenly spaced grid points. These parameters were selected through trial and
error, with the goal being that the potential at the left side is large enough to keep
the wave packet from crossing the simulation boundary, and ensuring that there is
enough room on the right side to allow for complete dissociation of the parts of
the wave packet that become excited into unbound states. An imaginary “gobbler”
potential Vgobbler was added at R = 14 [a0] to absorb unbound parts of the wave
packet. For reference, the form of the imaginary potential is as follows:
Vgobbler = −0.01iΘ (R −14) (R −14)2
Where Θ () is the Heaviside step function. A detailed discussion of this potential and
the ﬁne-tuning process that was used to create it is included in appendix H.2.
100
==PAGE:111==
CHAPTER 10. THE OH MORSE POTENTIAL

is know as Rabi-cycling or Rabi-ﬂopping [9], and occurs due to the anharmonicity of

spacing remains constant, so a constant resonant frequency will always be able to
excite the system into higher states, as was demonstrated analytically in section 7.5.1
of chapter 7.

the system and β = −8 × 10−8 [s−2].
It is clear to see that letting the frequency decrease over time will increase the
overall level of excitation compared to the results in ﬁgures 10.3a and 10.3b.
This underlines one of the main points of this thesis, which is that in order to
resonantly excite an anharmonic system, it is necessary to vary the driving frequency
as a function of time. In part II, the temporally chirped pulse was introduced. Here,
ﬁnally, is a clear motivation for the study and application of this concept.
The value of β in the previous example was chosen through trial and error. A β
that is too large will result in the frequency increasing too quickly for the system to
react. This limit can be investigated by performing a series of simulations where the
perturbing frequency once again has the form given by equation 10.4, and β assumes a
range of values. Additionally, the total running time T is adjusted for each simulation
103
==PAGE:114==
CHAPTER 10. THE OH MORSE POTENTIAL
so that when t = T, the instantaneous frequency will be equivalent to the transition
frequency between the two highest bound states, i.e. for the OH Morse potential:
ω0 + 2βT = E21 −E20
ℏ
(10.5)
In this way, the driving ﬁeld in each simulation will perform a sweep from the highest
to lowest resonant system frequencies, where the speed of the sweep depends on the
value of β. For a normalized wave packet, the dissociation probability as a function
of time Pdiss (t) can be expressed as 1−the sum occupancy of the N bound states:
Pdiss (t) = 1 −

The resulting dissociation probabilities when t = T for a series of 100 equally spaced

selected to show the greatest possible range of dissociation probabilities.
Remember, the values of β on the left side of the plot are equivalent to faster fre-
quency sweeps, and as β decreases the sweep becomes slower. The inverse correlation
between the speed of the sweep and the dissociation probability is clear to see; the
probability is almost 0 for the fastest sweeps, and close to 100 for the slowest. The
relation between the total running time T and β goes like T ∝β−1 , as can be seen
in equation 10.5 and in ﬁgure 10.5b, which shows T plotted as a function of β.
Figure 10.6a shows the dissociation probability at each value of β divided by the
total running time T. This plot indicates that the highest dissociation level per unit
time is achieved when β ≈−3 × 10−7 [s−2].

pulse. As stated in chapter 3, the electric ﬁeld of an experimental laser pulse can be

Part IV
Genetic Algorithm Optimizations
111
==PAGE:122==
Chapter 11
The Genetic Algorithm
11.1
Introduction
Part I of this thesis was a general introduction to the ﬁeld of femtochemistry. Part
II dealt with some of the theory behind spectral chirps and the inﬂuence they have
on the temporal pulse. Part III dealt with modeling the time evolution of quantum
systems, with a ﬁnal chapter in which the OH Morse potential was driven by electric
ﬁelds corresponding to quadratically chirped pulses. It was shown that linear temporal
chirps were able to more eﬀectively excite the system at certain pulse energies. Now, in
part IV, the goal will be to investigate ways to optimize temporal frequencies to drive
quantum systems into desired target states. This is a crux of femtochemistry. Genetic
algorithms [23] have been a topic of interest in this respect for some time; chapter
2 discussed how genetic, or evolutionary algorithms have been applied in numerous
experimental situations, and work has been done in theoretical ﬁelds of study as well
[33]. This chapter will describe the general principles behind the genetic algorithm.
For an in depth discussion and explanation of the particular implementation that was
used throughout this thesis, see appendix I.1.
112
==PAGE:123==
CHAPTER 11. THE GENETIC ALGORITHM
11.2
General Principles
Genetic algorithms have the potential to oﬀer robust solutions to optimization prob-
lems with:
• A large numbers of free parameters
• Systems in which there is little or no a priori knowledge about the underlying
mechanisms
• Systems that are highly nonlinear, stochastic, or non diﬀerentiable, or that
contain many local minima
The only requirement is that there be a way to evaluate the performance of a trial
solution. In a broad sense, genetic algorithms attempt to mimic the natural selec-
tion process when searching for an optimal solution. The basic procedure a genetic
algorithm uses to ﬁnd optimal parameters for a given problem is as follows:
1. Create an initial list of trial solutions with randomly selected parameters. Hence-
forth, each trial solution will be known as an “individual” and the sum total of
all the individuals on the list will be known as the “population”. The set of
parameters that describe each individual in the population is referred to as a
“genome”, much in the sense that an actual genetic sequence encodes all the
information to produce a real life organism.
2. Determine the “ﬁtness” of each individual in the population by using their pa-
rameters as an input to the optimization problem, and evaluate the performance
in relation to the goal output. An important point is that the ﬁtness criteria
are user deﬁned, i.e. there must be some way of delineating “good” solutions
from “bad” ones.
3. Create a new list, or “generation” of individuals from the current population.
There are a number of ways to do this, but the two most important factors
113
==PAGE:124==
CHAPTER 11. THE GENETIC ALGORITHM
are that individuals in the present generation with higher ﬁtness levels should
be more likely to pass on traits to the new generation (selection), and there
should be some element of randomness introduced to the genomes of the new
generation (mutation)
4. Repeat steps 2 −3 until the best ﬁtness level in the population converges, or
until a speciﬁed number of generations or time steps have passed.
In this way, the ﬁtness of each subsequent generation will (hopefully) be higher than
the previous generation, just as the forces of natural selection and mutation will
induce a population of animals to evolve traits that help ensure their survival.
A simple example of a genetic algorithm in action can be demonstrated by at-
tempting to minimize the Rastrigin function [11], which has the form:
Ras (x, y) = 20 + x2 + y2 −10 (cos (2πx) + cos (2πy))
(11.1)
This function has a global minimum at x = y = 0, surrounded by a large number of
local minima, as the sketch in ﬁgure 11.1 indicates.
minimizing the Rastrigin function means ﬁnding the value of x and y that will yield
the lowest value when plugged into equation 11.1. In terms of a genetic optimization,
this means that each individual will have a genome of length 2, corresponding to the
x and y values of a given trial solution. Since the goal is minimization, individuals
with x and y inputs that yield the lowest values will be scored the highest. Using
these rules, an initial population of 60 individuals was created, where each individual
was assigned an initial x and y value from −5 to 5. The Matlab genetic algorithm
implementation ga () (the details of which can be found in section I.1) was used to
evolve the population of solutions as per the procedure outlined in steps 1 −4. The
distributions of the individuals at generations 0,5, 10 and 30 are shown in ﬁgure 11.2.
The ﬁrst panel at generation 0 shows a random distribution of individuals within
the initial parameter boundaries. Note that none of the individuals are very close
114
==PAGE:125==
CHAPTER 11. THE GENETIC ALGORITHM
Figure 11.1: The non-convex Rastrigin function is a typical benchmark test for opti-
mization algorithms, as it contains one global minimum and a large number of local
minima
to the center.
Already at generation 5, a few of the individuals can be found in
the global minimum, and many are in nearby local minima. At generation 10, the
population distribution has become even more concentrated towards the center, and
ﬁnally at generation 30 every single individual has assumed the optimal value. In
practice, the optimized output of the algorithm is determined by the individual from
each generation with the highest ﬁtness. Going by this rule, the optimization in 11.2
could in principle already have been halted at generation 5, since the best performing
individuals here have already found values that are very close to the best possible.
It is not necessary for the starting population to be distributed near or around the
global minimum as it was in this example, however as a general rule, the further the
initial population distribution is from the optimal point, the larger the population
must be in order to adequately explore the parameter space.

==PAGE:126==
CHAPTER 11. THE GENETIC ALGORITHM

11.3
The Matlab ga() Function
The genetic algorithm employed throughout this thesis is the Matlab ga() implemen-
tation, which creates new generations using three types of “children” from the current
population:
• “elite” children are comprised of the best performing individuals in the current
population. These are passed directly to the new generation without alterations
• “mutant” children are selected individuals from current population that are
passed to the next generation with random changes introduced to their genome
• “crossover” children are created by combining the genomes of two diﬀerent in-
dividuals selected from the current population
116
==PAGE:127==
CHAPTER 11. THE GENETIC ALGORITHM
In general, the selection process for mutant and crossover children is stochastic and
favors higher ﬁtness individuals. The details behind the creation of these types of
children, along with other information about the Matlab implementation, can be
found in appendix I.1.
117
==PAGE:128==
Chapter 12
Target Pulse Shape Optimization
12.1
Introduction
This chapter will discuss the ﬁrst series of optimizations that were performed using
the genetic algorithm discussed in chapter 11. This will be a callback to part I of
this thesis, as the main topic will once again be spectral pulse shaping. Here, the
genetic algorithm will be used to predict the optimal spectral chirp required to form
a pulse of a desired temporal shape. In chapters 13 and 14, this will be taken one step
further, and spectral chirps will be optimized with the goal of controlling a quantum
system. The analysis in this chapter is meant as a “proof of concept” to show that
the genetic algorithm actually works for this purpose. The reason it makes sense
to test how well the algorithm can reproduce a target pulse shape is that, unlike
in the later optimizations, here it is easy to tell exactly how close the optimized
pulse is to a perfect solution. This chapter will start with a general overview of the
optimization setup and parameters, followed by a discussion of two ways the algorithm
was implemented, and a comparison of the results.
118
==PAGE:129==
CHAPTER 12. TARGET PULSE SHAPE OPTIMIZATION
12.2
Optimization Setup
The relationship between the spectral phase chirp function and the resulting shaped
pulse is complex.
Notwithstanding the results and analysis derived in part I, at
present time, there is no deterministic way to calculate the nature of the chirp function
required to achieve an arbitrary target pulse shape and frequency distribution [20].
Because of this, the genetic algorithm is a viable option for ﬁnding a suitable chirp
function to produce a desired temporal shape.
12.2.1
The Fitness Function
The ﬁrst task it to deﬁne a ﬁtness function. By default, the Matlab ga() function will
seek to ﬁnd parameters that minimize the ﬁtness function output. This means that
for a given chirp distribution θ (ω), spectral distribution ef(ω), shaped pulse function
fpulse(t) and target pulse ftarg(t), the ﬁtness of the chirp can be expressed as follows:
fpulse(t) = F−1 n
ef(ω) exp (iθ (ω))
o
ﬁtness =
R ∞
−∞|fpulse(t) −ftarg(t)|2 dt
2
R ∞
−∞|fpulse(t)|2
+ 1
This means that when fpulse(t) = ftarg(t) the ﬁtness function will be equal to 1, and if
no part of the pulses overlap, the ﬁtness function will be equal to 2. The ga() function
will therefore attempt to ﬁnd a chirp function that yields a ﬁtness level as close to 1
as possible.
12.2.2
Types of chirps
As discussed in section 5 of chapter 5, the discrete Fourier transform of a temporal
signal will result in a spectral distribution with frequencies that are binned into
discrete segments of size ∆ω. Due to the fact that the spectral intensity distribution
of a temporal Gaussian pulse can be approximated as having a ﬁnite spectral width,
119
==PAGE:130==
CHAPTER 12. TARGET PULSE SHAPE OPTIMIZATION
there exist a ﬁnite, discrete number of frequency bin components which will have
the ability to impact the pulse shape. It is of course true that the tail of a Gaussian
spectral distribution asymptotically approaches zero as it moves away from the central
pulse frequency, but in practice, a frequency window Fwindow with upper and lower
cutoﬀlimits is chosen and the phase is blanked i.e. set to 0 everywhere outside of this
region [37]. Given a Gaussian frequency distribution of form exp

−(ω−ω0)2
2σ2

, it was
found that selecting a frequency window width of 6σ, centered around the central
pulse frequency ω0, was suﬃciently wide for chirped frequencies beyond this limit to
not aﬀect the temporal pulse shapes in any noticeable way.
As stated, the discrete nature of the individual spectral components in Fourier
space puts an upper limit Pmax on the number of free optimization parameters :
Pmax =
1
∆ωFwindow = Fwindow
T
2π
Where T is the total width of the pulse time window. For example, in the ﬁrst of the
following analyses, The unshaped pulse was assigned a FWHM of 10 [fs], and the total
size of the time domain T was set to 300 [fs]. This results in 68 discrete bins in the
spectral domain, i.e. 68 maximum free parameters. Because this is a relatively large
number of variables, it was decided that applying a ﬁtted polynomial chirp function to
the spectral distribution might be a way to decrease the dimensionality of the problem
and thereby increase the eﬃciency of the algorithm. A 9th order polynomial spectral
chirp function was selected. The form of the spectral chirp function is therefore:
˜f (ω) = exp
 −α (ω −ω0)2
exp

9
X

φn (ω −ω0)(n)
#!
(12.1)
Where φ0−9 are the free parameters. The following sections will show the optimization
performance of ﬁtting pulse shapes by optimizing the phase of every discrete frequency
component individually, compared to the performance of the polynomial chirp method
that has just been outlined. All optimizations were performed with the following
120
==PAGE:131==
CHAPTER 12. TARGET PULSE SHAPE OPTIMIZATION
genetic parameters:
• A population size of 400 individuals
• A ﬁtness limit of 1.001
• A Convergence tolerance of 1 × 10−5 over 50 generations
• All other algorithm parameters were set to default.
12.3
The Discrete Chirp
For the ﬁrst tests, the series of spectrally chirped pulses from chapter 4 were selected as
target shapes for the algorithm to ﬁt to. The same 10 [fs] FWHM, 800 [nm] Gaussian
initial pulse was used to create the target and optimized pulses.
The pulse time
window T was set to 300 [fs]. The ﬁrst ﬁts were performed using the discrete chirp
distribution, i.e. the algorithm was given 68 free parameters, corresponding to the 68
frequency bins in the spectral domain.
It may seem natural to impose parameter boundaries on the search space of each
bin so the algorithm only ever tests values between 0 and 2π, however this was found
to produce convergence at local minima. The reason for this can be illustrated by the
following example: assuming a speciﬁc frequency component has an optimal value
of 0.01, the algorithm may try to approach this value from the bottom i.e from a
value close to 2π due to the periodicity of the phase. It is unlikely that a stochastic
mutation will be able to induce a jump from 2π to 0.01, so the ﬁtted value will get
“stuck” at or close to 2π but not be allowed to move higher to hit the optimum. (This
is a typical example of a “Hamming cliﬀ”, where two similar outputs lie very far from
each other in parameter space [12]). Because of this, the best results were achieved
with an initial population distribution between 0 and 2π, and no limits on the search
space.
121
==PAGE:132==
CHAPTER 12. TARGET PULSE SHAPE OPTIMIZATION
-150
-100
-50
0
50

150
scaled int. I (t)
0
