==PAGE:133==
CHAPTER 12. TARGET PULSE SHAPE OPTIMIZATION
As stated, the initial tests were performed using shaped pulses for which the
analytical chirp function is already known, i.e. the linear, quadratic, cubic and step
chirped pulses. All optimizations were performed at least twice in order to check the
consistency of the results.
The scaled target and ﬁtted temporal pulse intensities and their Wigner distribu-
tions are pictured in ﬁgures 12.1a and 12.1b, respectively. The bottom panel of ﬁgure
12.1a also shows the (scaled) discrepancy ∆I (t) = |fpulse (t) −ftarg (t)|2 between the
ﬁtted and target pulses. The agreement between the target and ﬁtted pulse is quite
good, and the discrepancy plot indicates that the error is uniformly distributed across
the time window of the pulse.
Figure 12.1c shows the phase chirp function of the target pulse, along with the
phase of the ﬁtted chirp function. The top panel shows the target chirp function
in blue, and the red curve is generated using the Matlab unwrap () function, which
unwraps a series of radian phase data points by changing absolute jumps between
two consecutive points that are larger than π to their 2π phase equivalent [37]. In
the bottom panel, the discrepancy ∆φ between the target and ﬁtted phase chirp is
plotted. ∆φ is calculated by reducing the phase of each datapoint in the target and
ﬁtted series to the 2π phase counterpart using Matlab’s wrapTo2Pi () function, and
subtracting the ﬁtted values from the target values. Once again it can be seen that
there is very good agreement between the target pulse chirp, and the chirp that the
genetic algorithm has chosen.
Figure 12.1d shows the best performing individual from each generation of the
optimization. The convergence is monotonically increasing, this is due to the elite
individuals that get passed from one generation to the next, ensuring that the best
performance in the next generation will never be worse than the previous generation.
As the plots show, the algorithm performs quite well. It is able to match the
target pulse with 99.9% conﬁdence after 3 minutes of calculation time. The opti-
mizations were performed on 32 cpu cores running in parallel. Genetic algorithms
123
==PAGE:134==
CHAPTER 12. TARGET PULSE SHAPE OPTIMIZATION
lend themselves quite well to computational parallelization, since the ﬁtness evalu-
ation of the individuals comprising a generation can be performed independently of
each other. It should not be assumed that running on 32 processors will make the
calculation 32× faster. In practice, it was found that parallel calculations performed
approximately 20× faster than the time required to perform the same optimization
on a single processor, i.e. the optimization process outlined in ﬁgure 12.1d would
have taken approximately 60 minutes without parallelization.
12.4
The Polynomial Chirp
Having shown that the genetic algorithm can indeed achieve good results when ﬁtting
68 parameters, a comparison was made by running the genetic algorithm on the same
target pulses, using the same parameters as before, except this time the 9th order
spectral polynomial ﬁtting method discussed in section 12.2.2 was employed instead.
As discussed in chapter 11, selecting reasonable boundaries on the initial param-
eters is an important aspect of eﬃcient optimization. Unlike the previous example,
where the initial parameter bounds were 0 −2π, a more reﬁned method of estimat-
ing the initial boundaries is required here. The reason for this is, as was discovered
in chapter 5, chirp coeﬃcients that vary by many orders of magnitude can produce
pulses with comparable widths, depending on the order of the polynomial chirp. The
initial ranges of the polynomial coeﬃcients were therefore estimated by approximat-
ing expressions for the relationship between pulse width and coeﬃcient magnitude for
spectral chirps of orders 2 −9 (note that orders 0 and 1 do not result in pulse broad-
ening. order 0, the phase term, was given initial parameter limits 0 −2π, and order
1, which shifts the pulse temporally, was given initial parameters equal to pulse time
window limits). This was accomplished by ﬁtting to a series of numerically generated
data points. An example of this is shown for orders 2 −4 in ﬁgure 12.2.

As in the case of the discrete chirp, parameter search bounds were not imposed
in order to maintain as much ﬂexibility as possible. As the results show, in this case
the ﬁtted pulse does not match up as well as in the discrete optimization. However,
the running time is drastically reduced. As ﬁgure 12.3d shows, here the optimization
only took 1.5 minutes, half as much time as the discrete optimization. This may not
seem like much, but remember that on one cpu, this represents a savings of roughly
30 minutes of calculation time. Considering the convergence plot in ﬁgure 12.3d, this
time could be reduced even further if the generation stall limit was decreased, as there
appears to be minimal improvement from generations 40 −90.
12.5
Comparison
In order to perform an in-depth comparison of the performance of the discrete and
polynomial ﬁtting algorithms, it was decided to test them against a series of target
pulses with parameterized complexity. This was achieved by shaping the target pulses
with a spectral chirp in the form of a sin function with amplitude 2π. By increasing
the number of oscillations of the sin function in the spectral domain across Fwindow,
it becomes easy to vary the complexity of the target pulse. This was done in order to
compare the performance of the discrete and polynomial optimization methods and
push them to their limits. Fits were made using both methods to temporal target
pulses shaped by spectral sin functions with 1, 2, 4, 8 and 16 oscillations within the
frequency window. Because a chirp of this nature will lead to a broader pulse, the
size of the time domain was increased to 1000 [fs]. Once again the optimized and
target pulses were shaped from 10 [fs] FWHM, 800 [nm] initial pulses, however the
size of the frequency chirp window was increased to 10σ. This led to a total of 375
discrete phases that could be adjusted by the discrete chirp method. The results of
this analysis, along with all the optimization results from the previous section, are
shown in table 12.1

Table 12.1: Comparison of the convergence time and accuracy of the results obtained
using the discrete and polynomial spectral chirp ﬁtting methods. All other genetic
algorithm parameters used in each optimization are identical to the ones discussed in
ﬁgures 12.1d and 12.3d.
Here it is again apparent that polynomial spectral chirp ﬁtting is a very eﬃcient
method for simple pulses with spectral chirps that can more readily be approximated
by a polynomial, but the quality of the results decrease quickly as the spectral chirp
of the target pulse becomes more complex.
Especially the π step chirp seems to
be diﬃcult for the polynomial method to handle, which is not unexpected since the
algorithm is trying to ﬁt a polynomial to a non-diﬀerentiable function. On the other
hand, the discrete ﬁtting method, while taking longer to converge, produces very
accurate results regardless of the pulse shape. Another interesting thing to note is
that when the number of free parameters in the discrete chirp optimization is increased
from 68−375, the running time only increases by approximately 4×. This underlines
how eﬀective the genetic algorithm is at handling searches in high-dimensional spaces,
considering the fact that even if one limits each discrete spectral chirp to 2 unique
values, there are still 10112 diﬀerent possible chirp combinations that can be created
with 375 frequency bins. Appendix J.1 contains plots of the temporal chirps, Wigner
distributions, spectral chirps, and convergence data for all the optimizations from
table 12.1 that haven’t been shown in this chapter.

==PAGE:139==
Chapter 13
The OH Morse Potential Revisited
13.1
Introduction
In chapter 12 it was demonstrated that the genetic algorithm can be used to ﬁt a
spectral chirp to achieve a desired target pulse shape. Now, it will be shown that the
genetic algorithm can also be used to determine the optimal frequency distributions
for controlling a quantum system. The focus in this chapter will be the dissociation
of the OH Morse potential introduced in chapter 10. To this end, there will be two
fundamental approaches to frequency optimization:
1. In section 13.2, the genetic algorithm will be used to construct optimal tem-
poral frequency distributions by ﬁtting a temporal frequency function. This is
reminiscent of investigations performed by Sharma et al in 2010 [33].
2. In section 13.3, the genetic algorithm will be used to construct optimized tem-
poral pulses by ﬁtting a spectral chirp function.
This is reminiscent of the
closed-loop system described in chapter 2.
129
==PAGE:140==
CHAPTER 13. THE OH MORSE POTENTIAL REVISITED
13.2
Temporal Pulse Frequency Optimization
An investigation of the optimal temporal frequency required to induce dissociation in
the OH Morse potential was performed by using a ﬁxed trapezoidal pulse envelope
function with an underlying temporal frequency chirp that varies as a function of a
ﬁtted polynomial of degree n. The next subsections will outline this methodology,
and results will be shown in which various optimization parameters are varied.
13.2.1
Methodology
A trapezoidal envelope pulse that starts at t = 0, with peak amplitude A0, can be
expressed as:

envelope shape, and the parameters of the genetic optimization

parameterized to have length 4A, where T1 = T3 = A and T2 = 2A, and peak

13.2.3
Initial Boundaries
As discussed in chapters 11 and 12, selection of reasonable initial parameter bound-
aries is an important aspect of the optimization process. As stated earlier, the pa-
rameters here are given by t0, c1, c2, . . . , cn in equation 13.1. Initial parameter bounds
on t0 are selected using the total pulse length:
0 < t0init < Tpulse
where Tpulse = T1 + T2 + T3. The initial bounds on c0 are given by
0 < c0init < 2π
Since c0 is merely a phase factor. The initial bounds on the coeﬃcients c1, c2, . . . , cn
are speciﬁed using the highest and lowest excitation frequencies of the OH Morse
potential ωmax and ωmin, and adjustable scaling parameter α:
αωmin
n (Tpulse)n−1 < cninit <
αωmax
n (Tpulse)n−1
The strategy is to select initial parameter boundaries such that the value of a single
parameter will not be able to cause the frequency to become lower than αωmin or
greater than αωmax by the time t = Tpulse. For example, for n = 2:
αωmin
2Tpulse
< c2init < αωmax
2Tpulse
132
==PAGE:143==
CHAPTER 13. THE OH MORSE POTENTIAL REVISITED
This means that if c2init is at the maximum possible value (and all other parameters
are = 0), the frequency function at t = Tpulse will be
ffreq (t) |t=Tpulse= exp


2Tpulse

And, therefore the instantaneous frequency will be
ωinst (Tpulse) = αωmax
By selecting initial parameter bounds in this manner, it is ensured that they will be
“in the ballpark” of the optimal values. In practice the algorithm was permitted to
search the parameter space beyond the initial boundaries. If a larger initial parameter
search space search is desired, the limits of the initial coeﬃcients can be adjusted
using the α scaling parameter. A ﬁnal remark on the conditions of the optimization
process is that nonlinear constraints were imposed on the solution space to ensure
that the instantaneous frequency never assumed negative values. The penalty method
of constrained optimization, which is discussed in appendix I.1, was employed for this
purpose. It must be noted that this does not always achieve perfect results, and the
instantaneous frequencies in some of the optimizations do assume slightly negative
values, however, as shall be seen, they are fairly insigniﬁcant and their impact was
deemed negligible. The ﬁtness function was deﬁned as:
Fitness =

Pdiss
Where Pdiss is the dissociation probability of the wave packet at t = Tpulse, given by

This means that in the best-case scenario, Fitness = 1, and in the the worst case,
Fitness = ∞. For reference, the genetic algorithm parameters in all the optimizations
in this section were as follows:
• A population of 5000 individuals
• 2 elite individuals were selected from each generation
• Of the remaining individuals comprising each generation, 90% were created as
crossover children, with the remaining 10% being mutant children
• The convergence tolerance was set to 1 × 10−3 over 50 generations
• The ﬁtness limit was set to 1.01. In practice this means that, barring no penal-
ties are incurred, the algorithm will terminate if the dissociation probability
≈99%. (this was never the case)
• All other parameters were set to the default
13.2.4
Order Variation
In the following subsection there will be a discussion of the ﬁrst analysis that was
performed using the aforementioned model of a trapezoidal envelope pulse envelope
and an optimized temporal frequency function. Here, the second moment width was
held constant at ∆t = 26 [fs], and the total energy was held constant at Epulse =
23.36
 J
cm2

. A series of optimizations were performed in which the order of the tem-
poral frequency function (equation 13.1) was varied from a ﬁrst to a sixth order
polynomial. For every optimization, the genetic algorithm was used to search for
polynomial coeﬃcient values that would maximize the dissociation probability when
the pulse drove the ground state of the OH Morse potential. The purpose of this
analysis is to investigate how varying the complexity of the temporal frequency chirp
will impact the performance. Until now, only linear temporal frequency distributions

Figure 13.4
Both ﬁgures show “ladder climbing” between adjacent states that is indicative of
resonant transitions. The fourth order plot shows that the wave packet remains more
focused in a few adjacent states as it moves up the state/energy ladder. Examining
ﬁgure 13.3, it appears that this focusing is achieved by starting with a frequency
that is slightly lower than the ground state transition level. This is interesting, and
may be due to the fact that it is possible to induce partial excitation in the Morse
ground state with frequencies that are slightly non-resonant. In this way, performing a
sweep of frequencies that are close to the resonant ground state transition frequency
before starting to climb the ladder may induce more eﬀective excitation. Another
interesting part of the state distributions in ﬁgures 13.4a and 13.4b are the faint
secondary resonant transitions that seem to appear in both ﬁgures at around 80 [fs].
The fact that they appear on both sets of data is intriguing, however at present
time it is unknown if these are simply numerical artifacts, or represent some inherent
physical principle at work.

13.2.5
Width Variation
Having demonstrated the eﬀect that optimized temporal frequency distributions with
varying levels of complexity will have on the dissociation of the Morse potential
ground state, the next series of optimizations that were performed involved variation
of the pulse widths, keeping the total pulse energy constant at Epulse = 23.36
 J
cm2

,
and optimizing each pulse using a sixth order frequency function in the exponential
argument of equation 13.1. This is reminiscent of the analysis performed in chapter
10 in which the Morse potential ground state was driven by a series of quadratically
chirped pulses. The diﬀerence here is that now the temporal frequency chirp is no
longer simply linear. Figure 13.5a shows the results of performing 16 optimizations
for 16 equally spaced pulse widths from 6 [fs] to 96 [fs] .
The bar plot shows that the shortest pulses are not able to produce any apprecia-
ble dissociation. As the pulse width increases from 6−30 [fs], the level of dissociation
quickly increases as well. After this, the dissociation probability levels oﬀ, and al-
138
==PAGE:149==
CHAPTER 13. THE OH MORSE POTENTIAL REVISITED
though there appear to be oscillations from one width to the next, the overall level
of dissociation appears to remain fairly constant for pulse widths from 30 −96 [fs].
There is a fairly large variation in the overall shapes of the pulses across this range of
widths, as ﬁgure 13.5b indicates. The fact that such a large variation of pulse shapes
will produce comparable levels of dissociation is interesting. It seems that for pulses
of equivalent energies, there is a more or less even trade oﬀbetween peak intensity
and width, at least within the ranges examined here.
13.3
Spectral Phase Chirp Optimization
In chapter 12 it was shown that the genetic algorithm can be used to ﬁt target pulse
shapes via a spectral chirp, and in section 13.2 it has been shown that the algorithm
is also able to optimize a temporal frequency function to dissociate the OH Morse
potential ground state. Now it is time to take the ﬁnal step; optimizing a spectral
phase chirp in order to generate a shaped temporal pulse that can control a quantum
system. In the following subsections, it will be shown that the genetic algorithm
can be used to construct such optimized spectral chirps. First, there will be short
discussion of the techniques that were employed in order to make this possible.
13.3.1
Constraints
Due to the ﬁnite size of the time domain and periodic boundary conditions of the
DFT, it was found that simply allowing the genetic algorithm to manipulate the
spectral phase until an optimum result was found would most often result in unphys-
ical temporal pulse shapes, i.e. the pulses tended to be so wide that they interfered
with themselves due to periodic boundaries. One possible solution to this problem
would be to simply increase the size of the pulse time window T. However, since the
optimal pulse can potentially be extremely broad, this can lead to a huge compu-
tational workload. There are also physical/experimental arguments for limiting the
139
==PAGE:150==
CHAPTER 13. THE OH MORSE POTENTIAL REVISITED
pulse width:
• The ﬁnite frequency resolution in the 4f line pulse shaper limits the width of
the shaped pulse window.
• It has been found experimentally [34] that if a system is driven for long periods
of time, dissipation will occur via e.g. thermal coupling.
It was therefore decided that some kind of constrained optimization was required.
Matlab’s genetic algorithm implementation contains built in linear and nonlinear
constraint functions. However, these were not found to be useful for this particular
purpose, perhaps due to the highly nonlinear relationship between pulse widths and
chirp functions, especially for discrete chirp distributions. Instead, a custom con-
straint function was written which would invoke a penalty factor that increases as
a function of how far the trial pulse width is from the speciﬁed target width. The
design and implementation of this function is discussed in detail in appendix K.2.
13.3.2
Optimization Parameters
As in the pulse target shape ﬁtting performed in chapter 12, the genetic algorithm was
used to optimize both discrete and polynomial spectral chirp functions. The initial
parameter boundaries on the polynomial chirps were selected in the same manner as
discussed in subsection 12.4 of chapter 12, however in this case the parameter bound-
aries were selected so the maximum width was equal to the maximum feasible pulse
width within the pulse time window T. (for an explanation of how this quantity is
deﬁned, see appendix K.2) Unlike the polynomial spectral chirp optimizations per-
formed in chapter 12, the initial parameter boundaries on the chirp coeﬃcients were
also imposed as boundaries on the search space.
In addition to varying the spectral chirp phases, the algorithm was also permitted
to vary the central pulse frequency. As was the case for the quadratic spectral chirp
analysis performed in section 10.6 of chapter 10, the lower limit was imposed on the
140
==PAGE:151==
CHAPTER 13. THE OH MORSE POTENTIAL REVISITED
search space using the same line of reasoning as discussed in section 3.4 of chapter
3, to avoid negative frequencies. The upper limit was selected as twice the highest
transition frequency of the OH Morse potential.
As has always been the case up to this point, the FWHM of the initial, unshaped
pulse was selected to be 10 [fs] , once again following the reasoning outlined in sub-
section 10.6 and illustrated in ﬁgure 10.7. The ﬁtness function was deﬁned as follows:
ﬁtness = penalty
Pdiss
Where Pdiss is the dissociation probability, given by equation 13.5, and “penalty” is
the penalty on the pulse width calculated by the function described in appendix K.2.
For reference, the genetic algorithm parameters for all optimizations in this section
were as as follows:
• A population of 5000 individuals
• 2 elite individuals were selected from each generation
• Of the remaining individuals comprising each generation, 90% were created as
crossover children, with the remaining 10% being mutant children
• The convergence tolerance was set to 1 × 10−3 over 50 generations
• The ﬁtness limit was set to 1.01. In practice this means that, barring no penal-
ties are incurred, the algorithm will terminate if the dissociation probability
∼99%. (this was never the case)
• All other parameters were set to the default
13.3.3
Chirp Types
An investigation was performed by generating optimized pulses with energy Epulse =
23.36
 J
cm2

and a target second moment width of ∆t = 26 [fs]. These parameters

==PAGE:152==
CHAPTER 13. THE OH MORSE POTENTIAL REVISITED
were chosen to match the trapezoidal envelope pulses analyzed in subsection 13.2.4.
The pulses were centered in a time window T of width 300 [fs], and the size of the
chirped region around the center of the pulse frequency distribution was set to 10σ.
This resulted in 112 independent frequency phases that can be manipulated in the
spectral domain.
As explained earlier, the spectral chirps were created in two ways; by ﬁtting the
phase of each discrete frequency bin, and by ﬁtting the coeﬃcients of a polynomial
spectral chirp function with the general form:

Figure 13.6 shows the resulting dissociation levels for pulses shaped by optimized
quadratic, cubic, quartic, and discrete spectral chirp functions.
Because this op-
timization method could conceivably be applied to an experimental situation, the
optimization running times have been plotted as well for reference.
Attempts were made to ﬁt polynomial spectral chirps of orders higher than quartic,
however it was found that the algorithm produced signiﬁcantly diﬀerent pulses and
dissociation levels every time it was run. This is indicative of premature convergence
at local minima. Because of this issue, only polynomial chirps up to quartic order
will be analyzed.
The discrete chirp optimizations also showed variations in the
temporal pulse shapes over successive optimizations, however the dissociation levels
were consistently found to be around 18 −22 [%]. It was found that the algorithm
performed best when the width constraint function was relaxed slightly. Because of
this, the widths of the generated pulses did not always exactly line up with their target
positions. However it will be seen that these discrepancies were not signiﬁcant, and
none of the pulses had problems with self-interference at the time window boundaries.
Figure 13.6 shows that the discrete chirp achieves the highest dissociation level
of ∼22% . However, in terms of computational eﬃciency the quartic chirp performs

Table 13.1: The dissociation levels of an un-chirped pulse with central frequency equal

The ﬁrst panels of both of these ﬁgures show the amplitude of the temporal pulse
as a function of time. The solid black lines indicate the positions of the target locations
for the left and right edges of the pulse second moment widths, and the dashed red
lines indicate the edge locations of the optimized pulses. Both plots show that the
target and actual edge locations are close to each other, which indicates that the
attempt to impose constraints on the pulse widths has been mostly successful.
For a more detailed performance comparison, table 13.1 gives the dissociation
levels of the four diﬀerent optimized pulses to 2 signiﬁcant digits, and compares
them to the dissociation achieved by the corresponding un-chirped pulse with central
frequency equivalent to the 0 −1 Morse transition frequency. The second column
shows the relative improvement in performance of the pulses W.R.T. the un-chirped
pulse. It can be seen that even for the simple quadratic chirp, the dissociation is over
4 orders of magnitude greater than that of the un-chirped pulse.
The Wigner distribution of the quartic pulse indicates that at the beginning of
the pulse, the energy is located near the 0 −1 transition frequency of the Morse
potential at ∼113 [THz]. As time progresses, the instantaneous temporal frequency
of the pulse decreases. It was shown in chapter 10 that this is a necessary condition
for resonant excitation of the anharmonic Morse potential.
The Wigner distribution also suggests that the optimized pulse starts oﬀwith a
relatively slowly increasing temporal frequency at lower amplitudes, and as the pulse
amplitude increases, so does the rate of frequency increase, until there is giant “kick”
146
==PAGE:157==
CHAPTER 13. THE OH MORSE POTENTIAL REVISITED
of amplitude concentrated at around ∼20 [fs] and 60 −70 [THz]. Recall that in sub-
section 7.5.1 of chapter 7, two fundamental ways of exciting a system were discussed;
resonant driving frequencies that gradually “climb the rungs”, and high amplitude
oscillations that “kick” the system into higher levels. Here, it seems, the algorithm
has found a way to exploit both of these methods to maximize the dissociation. This
behavior is also reﬂected in the state occupancy of the system, i.e. panels 3 −4 of
ﬁgure 13.7, where the resonant ladder climbing is observed to begin slowly at around
∼−100 [fs], and from around ∼−100 [fs] to ∼−20 [fs], the rate of climb increases in
accordance with the increasing rate of frequency change, until the kick at ∼20 [fs]
gives the system the last ﬁnal push and parts of the wave packet become dissociated,
as indicated by the sudden increase in dissociation probability at ∼25 [fs] that can
be observed in the third panel.
Compared to the quarticly chirped pulse, the discrete chirped pulse is much more
complex. The Wigner distribution shows that the temporal frequency chirp follows
a slight negative trend, and the pulse frequencies are in general located in the same
region as those of the quarticly chirped pulse i.e. the higher region of the OH Morse
transition frequencies.
The discrete spectral chirp temporal pulse shown in the ﬁrst panel of ﬁgure 13.8
contains a series of amplitude “bumps”, and upon inspection of the state occupancies,
it appears that each bump corresponds more or less to a diﬀerent excitation “regime” of
the system. For example; note how the small bump that appears at around ∼−50 [fs]
to ∼−30 [fs] corresponds to a sudden increase in the rate at which the ground state
is being emptied, which can be clearly seen in the ground state occupancy in panel
3. Moreover, the small bump at around ∼50 [fs] to ∼60 [fs] seems to be responsible
for producing the majority of the dissociation. The fact that the optimized discrete
spectral chirp seems to have divided the temporal pulse into small “local” pulses of
this nature is intriguing, and the increase in performance achieved by this technique
seems to warrant future investigation.

The spectral pulse distributions, and the 0 −2π equivalent optimized phase shifts
for the discrete frequency components of the optimized quadratic, cubic, quartic, and
discrete spectral chirps are shown in ﬁgure 13.9. In addition to this, the explicit
values of the optimized chirp coeﬃcients for the quadratic, cubic and quartic spectral
chirps are shown in table 13.2.
Remember that the polynomial chirp functions are smooth curves, so the jagged
appearance of the plotted phase chirps in ﬁgure 13.9 are due to the fact that the chirp
functions have been folded back onto themselves so they will only lie between 0 and
2π. Bearing this in mind, it appears that all the chirp distributions appear to have
“humps” located at or near the central pulse frequencies. This makes sense, consider-
ing the fact that humps like this are similar to the form of a negative quadratic chirp
coeﬃcient, which is a general requirement for creating negative temporal frequency
chirp in the shaped pulse. Indeed, the φ2 chirp coeﬃcient of the quadratic order chirp
in table 13.1 shows that the best performance is achieved with a negative chirp coeﬃ-
cient. The cubic order chirp coeﬃcients show that the optimal pulse here will contain
a blend of cubic and quadratic coeﬃcients. The quartic order chirp coeﬃcients are
perhaps the most interesting, as it seems that here the algorithm has decided that the
temporal chirp produced by a negative quartic coeﬃcient is more eﬀective than the
temporal chirp produced by the quadratic coeﬃcient, as φ2 has been greatly reduced
compared to the quadratic and cubic examples, and φ4 is relatively large.

13.3.4
Width Variation
As in the previous analysis performed in section 13.2, the genetic algorithm was used
to construct pulses of various widths using optimized spectral chirps, to investigate
the impact that the pulse width may have on the dissociation level. To give the
algorithm the best chances of maximizing the dissociation, discrete chirps were ﬁtted.
The widths, corresponding dissociation probabilities, and optimization running times
are plotted in ﬁgure 13.10:
In order to increase the pulse width, it becomes necessary to increase the width of
the temporal pulse window T (and thereby the quantum simulation time). The 28 [fs],
55 [fs], 101 [fs] and 125 [fs] pulses required 300 [fs], 600 [fs], 900 [fs] and 1200 [fs] time
windows, respectively. This leads to a proportional increase in the number of discrete
frequency phase components available to chirp in each optimization, (112, 224, 336
and 448, respectively) For these reasons, the optimization time increases signiﬁcantly
for wider pulses. The plots show that the maximum dissociation is achieved with
the 101 [fs] pulse, where the performance is comparable to that of the trapezoidal
pulses from section 13.2. The temporal amplitude, Wigner distribution, and state
150
==PAGE:161==
CHAPTER 13. THE OH MORSE POTENTIAL REVISITED
occupancy for the optimized 101 [fs] pulse simulation can be located in appendix K.3.
13.4
Optimization Analysis
In the previous two sections, it was shown that the genetic algorithm is capable of
creating optimized pulses that can dramatically increase the dissociation of the OH
Morse potential ground state, when compared with un-optimized pulses. The best
performing trapezoidal pulse was able to achieve ∼65% dissociation at a second
moment pulse width ∆t ≈85 [fs]. The best performing optimized spectral chirp pulse
was able to achieve ∼47% dissociation at a second moment width of ∆t ≈101 [fs].

As with any optimization of a complex system, it can be diﬃcult to tell whether
or not the global minimum has actually been located. One case where this is feasible,
is the quadratic spectral chirp optimization. The reason for this is apparent upon
151
==PAGE:162==
CHAPTER 13. THE OH MORSE POTENTIAL REVISITED
inspection of equation 6.2 from chapter 6, where the second moment pulse width is
shown to be related to the quadratic chirp factor φ2 in the following way:
∆t =
r
α2 + φ2
2
α
Where √α denotes the second moment width of the un-chirped pulse. Here it can
be seen that for a given initial width
√
α′ and width constraint ∆t′, there are only
two possible values of φ2 that satisfy the equation, given by ±φ′
2. In general, it is
clear that the algorithm will select −φ′
2, since this will produce a negative temporal
chirp. Additionally, the linear phase chirp φ1 will have to be ∼0 as if it isn’t, the
pulse will not be centered at t = 0, which will violate the pulse width constraints.
This means that the only actual “free” parameters that the optimization has to work
with are the central pulse frequency ω0, and the constant chirp phase φ0. Finding
the global minimum of these two variables can be accomplished via parametric sweep
of the central pulse frequency and constant chirp phase. This is visualized in the 2D
plot shown in ﬁgure 13.11.
The plot shows the location of the optimized parameters selected by the genetic
algorithm (X), along with the location of the global maximum as determined by
the parametric sweep of the free parameters ω0 and φ0 (O). As can be seen, these
two values are nearly directly on top of each other, which indicates that the genetic
algorithm did indeed converge at the global optimum. This is an excellent sign, and
indicates that it is likely that the higher order chirps have converged on the optimal
parameters as well.
152
==PAGE:163==
Chapter 14
The Double Well
14.1
Introduction
So far, the only model system that has been considered is the OH Morse potential.
This model has been subject to a number of analyses, and in the process a number
of optimization techniques have been developed. Now some of these techniques will
be applied to a diﬀerent kind of system; the asymmetric double well potential. The
double well potential is a simple model that captures the dynamics of a system in
which there are two energy minima, i.e. it can exhibit bistability. This behavior can
be found in e.g. some rotational molecular bonds, where multiple stable conﬁgurations
(i.e. isomers) can exist at local energy minima as a function of the rotational angle
[22].
In the subsequent analysis, the goal will not be to derive a model that is
100% realistic, but rather to focus on the general dynamics of a bistable quantum
system. As such, the model will be a simpliﬁed 1D potential that varies as a function
of intermolecular distance, as is used in [31].
The potential function is a quartic
polynomial of the following form:
V (r) =
 0.5R4 −1 × 10−5R3 −15R2 −12.02R + 160.2

5 × 10−4
(14.1)
153
==PAGE:164==
CHAPTER 14. THE DOUBLE WELL
Where R is the intermolecular distance and m = 1836 [me] is the proton mass. The
molecular dipole moment of the system is a simple linear function of R:
µ (R) = µ1R
Where µ1 = 1 [e]. The following three sections will include a brief discussion of the
simulation parameter optimizations, as well as a series of genetic optimizations similar
to the ones performed on the OH Morse potential in chapter 13, in which pulses will
be optimized to induce a population transfer from the right well ψ0 ground state to the
ψ12 left well energy minimum state. For reference, all genetic algorithm parameters
such as population, elite count and crossover rate used in the next two analyses are
identical to the parameters used for the optimizations in chapter 13.
14.2
Parameters
As discussed in chapter 10, the optimal simulation parameters depend on the charac-
teristic energies and frequencies of the system in question. Because of this, an analysis
identical to the one performed on the OH model was performed on the double well
system in order to select optimal simulation parameters. The details of this analysis
can be located in appendix L.1.
The stationary eigenstates of the double well potential were calculated using the
FGH method described in chapter 8. It was found that the local energy minimum
state at the left well corresponds to the 12th eigenstate of the system, as shown in
ﬁgure 14.1:
The optimization goal will therefore be, as stated, to maximize the “population
transfer” from ψ0 →ψ12. The general ﬁtness function is therefore:
ﬁtness =

produces state transitions both up and down the energy ladder.
14.3
Temporal Pulse Frequency Optimization
A series of optimizations were performed using pulses consisting of trapezoidal en-
velope functions and optimized temporal frequency functions, just as in section 13.2
of chapter 13. As before, all pulse energies will be the same throughout the analysis
in order to facilitate comparison of other parameters. An additional argument for
keeping the energy constant is the fact that the molecular dipole moment of the dou-
ble well system has been approximated by a linear function. This means that if the
pulse amplitude becomes very large it may lead to an unphysical coupling between

Figure 14.3: Instantaneous frequency functions for the ﬁrst, second, third and fourth
order polynomial functions optimized to maximize the ψ0 →ψ12 population transfer

A feature to note in the two higher order curves is that until t ≈250 [fs] the
instantaneous frequencies are much higher than the resonant frequencies of the sys-
tem. This behavior is interpreted as a form of “holding pattern”, as the non-resonant
frequencies will not impact the system. The reasoning behind this is that if too much
energy is absorbed over the duration of the pulse, it will become diﬃcult to descend
the energy rungs to the bottom of the left well after crossing the potential barrier.
This indicates that using a very long or energetic pulse may be counterproductive to
inducing eﬀective population transfer.
The system dynamics when driven by the fourth order pulse were plotted in order
to further illuminate the mechanism behind the population transfer. The wave func-
tion norm as a function of position and time is shown in ﬁgure 14.4, and the energy
expectation value as a function of time is plotted in ﬁgure 14.5.
Figure 14.4 clearly shows how the wave packet, which starts as a Gaussian dis-
tribution centered at ≈4 [a0], remains well localized as it is gradually excited into

14.3.2
Width Variation
As in the analysis of the Morse potential, a series of sixth order optimizations were
performed in which the pulse energy was once again kept constant at Epulse ≈0.7
 J
cm2

and the second moment width of the envelope function was varied from 6 [fs] to 97 [fs].
The resulting population transfer as a function of width is plotted in ﬁgure 14.7a.
The bar plot indicates that after the pulse reaches a certain minimum width, fur-
ther increase seems to have little impact on the results, although there is a slight neg-
ative correlation between performance and width from ∆t ≈50 [fs] to ∆t ≈100 [fs].
This is perhaps due to the idea posited earlier that a very long transition process
will result in the wave packet becoming more spread out, which in the end makes it
more diﬃcult to focus it back down into a single state. To shed some more light on
160
==PAGE:171==
CHAPTER 14. THE DOUBLE WELL
the dynamics of longer vs. shorter pulses, plots of the system energies as functions
of time for pulses of second moment width 47 [fs], 60 [fs], 79 [fs] and 97 [fs] are shown
in ﬁgure 14.7b. The overall shapes of the heating/cooling curves across this range of
widths are quite similar, although the transition process appears to happen slightly
faster in the shorter pulses, with a higher maximum energy peak, most likely due to
the higher amplitude of the driving ﬁeld. This indicates that the optimal transfer
dynamics are mostly dependent on the system and less so on the pulse parameters.
14.4
Spectral Phase Chirp Optimization
Following the procedure in section 13.3 from chapter 13, the genetic algorithm was
used to create optimized spectral chirps with the goal of producing a temporal pulse
that would maximize the population transfer from eigenstates ψ0 →ψ12 of the double
well potential. The method of pulse width constraint and optimization parameters
were identical to those of the Morse potential optimizations performed in section 13.3.
A key diﬀerence, however, was that in this case the unshaped pulse was assigned a
FWHM of 30 [fs] instead of the shorter 10 [fs] pulse that was used for the Morse
potential. This is because the lower excitation frequencies present in the double well
system require a narrower spectral distribution to be reached eﬀectively, as per the
argumentation outlined in subsection 10.6 of chapter 10, and in particular, illustrated
in ﬁgure 10.7. All optimized pulses had total energy Epulse ≈0.7
 J
cm2

, which is the
same as was used in the temporal chirp optimizations from section 14.3.
14.4.1

The genetic algorithm was used to optimize quadratic, cubic, quartic, and discrete
spectral chirps. Figure 14.8 shows the performance and computation time for the four
types of spectrally chirped pulse, each of which were optimized with second moment
target width ∆t = 104 [fs]. In each case the pulse time window T was set to 1200 [fs],

It appears that the success rate increases with the complexity of the spectral
chirp, just as in the case of the optimized chirps for the Morse potential in chapter

transfer induced by the optimized quadratic, cubic, quartic and discrete spectral
chirps, compared to the performance of a 30 FWHM un-chirped pulse with the same
energy, and a central pulse frequency equivalent to the 0 −1 transition frequency of
the double well potential.
Here it is clear to see how eﬀective the optimization process has been; even when
applying a simple quadratic chirp function to optimize the temporal pulse, the popula-
tion transfer will increase by over 18 orders of magnitude compared to the un-chirped
pulse. It is interesting to note that the quadratic and cubic spectral chirps perform

Figures 14.9 and 14.10 show the temporal pulse amplitudes, Wigner distributions,
and state occupancies of the quartic and discrete optimized chirps.
The state occupancy plot for the quartic chirped pulse shows fast and focused
ladder climbing from the ground state to approximately the 55th −60th eigenstates
over a period of approximately 100 [fs]. After this, the pulse seems to have trouble
keeping the wave packet focused in adjacent eigenstates as it descends the ladder,
and it becomes spread out over a larger distribution of states by the time t = Tpulse,
leading to a population transfer of 33%.
The state occupancy plot for the discrete chirped pulse shows a slightly slower,
less focused initial ladder climb from ∼−400 [fs] to ∼−80 [fs], after which a very
fast and focused climb, transition and descent occurs between ∼−80 [fs] and ∼0 [fs].
Directly after this transition, the wave packet has become spread out over a few
eigenstates, and at this point the pulse is somehow able to focus a signiﬁcant portion
of the wave packet into the target state from ∼0 [fs] to ∼200 [fs], resulting in a
population transfer of 66%, approximately double that of the quartic pulse.
The cooling and focusing behaviors that occur near the end of the pulses appear
to be important factors that have a large inﬂuence on the overall success rate. The
temporal oscillations present in the discrete chirp pulse from ∼0 [fs] to ∼200 [fs],

where the cooling occurs, are quite complex and it is diﬃcult to see exactly how the

be interesting to investigate the mechanisms involved behind getting a “spread-out”

Figure 14.11 shows the discrete 2π equivalent phase chirps of the quadratic, cubic,
quartic and discrete optimized spectral chirps for the double well system. Addition-
ally, the explicit values of the polynomial chirp coeﬃcients of the quadratic, cubic
and quartic spectral chirps are shown in table 14.2.
As noted earlier, the quadratic and cubic chirp functions are very similar, as they
are both dominated by large negative quadratic chirp coeﬃcients. The quartic func-
tion is interesting because the quadratic φ2 coeﬃcient and the quartic φ4 coeﬃcient
have opposite signs, which indicates that the temporal pulse may contain both pos-
itive and negative frequency chirps. This is perhaps not surprising, considering that
the dynamics of the system require transitions both up and down the energy ladder.
The form of the discrete spectral chirp shown in the last panel of ﬁgure 14.11 is
complex, however it appears that close to the central frequency the phase shift does
not jump by large increments from one frequency component to the next. This is
interesting because it suggests that it may be possible to approximate the discrete
spectral chirp with a smooth function.

probability was investigated using a series of optimized discrete chirps. The initial

As before, it was necessary to vary the size of the pulse time window T in order

27 [fs], 52 [fs], 78 [fs], and 104 [fs] pulses required time windows of 300 [fs], 600 [fs],
900 [fs] and 1200 [fs], respectively. Using a spectrally chirped region width of 10σ,
this translates into 37, 74, 111 and 149 discrete frequency components, respectively.
Figure 14.12 shows that the population transfer decreases drastically for short pulses,
and the best results are achieved using a pulse width of 78 [fs]. The general dynamics
here are reﬂected by the plot in ﬁgure 14.7a, in which the optimal transfer appears
to happen when the pulse is long enough to create the necessary heating/cooling
dynamics, but not so long that the wave packet becomes too spread out while it is
being transferred. (The data for the best performing 78 [fs] pulse can be found in
appendix L.2)

==PAGE:179==
Part V
General Discussion and Conclusion
169
==PAGE:180==
Chapter 15
General Discussion
The main topics of this thesis are distributed over three parts; spectral pulse shaping,
time-dependent quantum mechanics, and genetic optimization of pulses. The results
from each particular investigation have already been discussed throughout the body
of this thesis, so this chapter will instead be a generalized overview.
15.1
Pulse Shaping
The analytic and numerical results for the temporal pulses created by spectral phase
chirps performed in part II have helped to gain an understanding of a few general
relationships between the form of the spectral chirp and the temporal pulse. As with
any theoretical analysis, it is important to consider the results from the perspective
of the “real” world.
When spectral chirp pulse shaping is being performed in an
experimental situation, e.g. when using a 4f-line pulse shaper, there are a number of
factors that can cause the shaped pulse to diﬀer from the theoretical predictions.
The 4f-line pulse shaper induces spectral chirp via the LC-SLM described and
pictured in ﬁgures 2.2b and 2.2a, in chapter 2. This causes the experimental spectral
chirp to diﬀer from the analytic in two important ways:
1. The construction of the LC-SLM means that a polynomial spectral chirp func-
170
==PAGE:181==
CHAPTER 15. GENERAL DISCUSSION
tion will need to be represented on a series of discrete grid points, corresponding
to the pixel locations.
2. There is a “dead zone” between the pixel locations in the LC-SLM mask, which
is represented by the 3 [µm] white lines between the grey areas in ﬁgure 2.2a
from chapter 2. This dead zone means that there will always be some parts of
the spectral pulse that “are out of reach” of the pulse shaper, and that some of
the initial pulse intensity will be absorbed.
In addition to the ﬁnite spectral resolution imposed by the LC-SLM, there is a phys-
ically imposed limit caused by spatial-temporal coupling. This concept, which does
not fall within the scope of this thesis, is described in [2]. As was discussed in chap-
ter 5, a ﬁnite spectral resolution puts a limit on the complexity of the phase chirp
function and the width of the time window in which shaped pulses may exist.
While the discrete Fourier transform, which is used extensively throughout this
thesis, has the properties of ﬁnite spectral resolution, the next step in the analysis
could be to include the LC-SLM and the spatial temporal coupling eﬀects in the theo-
retical model when calculating the temporal pulse shape. This may lead to additional
insights about the relationship between the spectral phase chirp and temporal pulse
that do not present themselves when working with the reverse Fourier transform.
15.2
Time-Dependent Quantum Mechanics
The ﬁrst sections of this part were primarily concerned with describing the method-
ology behind simulating a molecule in a time-dependent electric ﬁeld. These are deep
concepts, and an in-depth discussion of any one of them could easily be the topic of
an entire thesis in itself.
In chapter 10, these methodologies were applied to the OH Morse potential. The
molecular models employed in this thesis were designed to be as simple as possible
while still capturing some important dynamics. These dynamics allowed for some
171
==PAGE:182==
CHAPTER 15. GENERAL DISCUSSION
accurate general predictions; for example in chapter 10 it was shown that the anhar-
monicity of the simple Morse potential model means that pulses optimized to disso-
ciate the system will in general be characterized by a negative temporal frequency
chirp, which has also been demonstrated experimentally [34].
There are a number of ways to reﬁne the simulations that warrant future investi-
gation:
1. Expand the model to include 3 or more atoms
2. Allowing for transitions to and from higher electronic states
3. Including the transition dipole moment [22] in the ﬁeld/molecule interaction.
Increasing the complexity of the model increases the computation time. Therefore, for
the sake of increased computational eﬃciency, an in-depth analysis and investigation
of other methods for solving the TDSE such, as the Chebyshev method described
in [35] and [18], may be in order. In this respect, implementing the code in a more
optimized language than Matlab may also be beneﬁcial.
15.3
Genetic Optimizations
The genetic optimizations performed in this part of the thesis demonstrated that the
algorithm can be extremely eﬀective for the purpose of optimizing an electric ﬁeld for
quantum control. One weakness of the method is the calculation time; most of the
optimizations in this thesis would be unfeasible to perform on a single computer (this
is perhaps not a problem in an experimental situation with a real molecular reaction,
since nature can “solve” the TDSE in real time).
The genetic algorithm does not always oﬀer clues about the underlying mechanism
of the system it is optimizing. For example, the optimized discrete spectral chirps in
chapters 13 and 14 are so complex that it becomes diﬃcult to say anything meaningful
about them, other than the fact that they get the job done.
To use the genetic
172
==PAGE:183==
CHAPTER 15. GENERAL DISCUSSION
algorithm as a tool for analysis, one must design optimization problems where it is
possible to interpret the results. In this thesis, this was accomplished by e.g. using
the algorithm to optimize a temporal frequency function where parameters such as
the pulse energy, width, and temporal envelope shape were held ﬁxed. By limiting
the inﬂuence of the algorithm in this way, it becomes easier to isolate important
characteristics of the optimized pulse, e.g. in chapter 14 it was discovered that there
is a system-dependent optimal time frame for achieving successful population transfer
that does not signiﬁcantly change when the pulse amplitude and width is altered.
Another issue with the genetic algorithm is that it is not always certain whether
or not it has truly found the optimal parameters.
This was investigated W.R.T.
the optimized quadratic spectral chirp in section 13.4 of chapter 13, and the results
were promising, but it was also found that the algorithm sometimes converged on
diﬀerent results for optimizations with large numbers of variables e.g. the discrete
chirp optimizations. This indicates a problem with convergence at local minima. One
way around this is simply to run the algorithm multiple times and choose the best
result. There are other, more reﬁned ways of handling the local minima problem; one
interesting idea is using a “multi-population” genetic algorithm [27], which increases
the genetic diversity of the search by evolving a number of “local” populations that
are isolated from one another, and allowing some individuals to migrate between
populations. Another solution could be to use “seed” populations, i.e. feeding the
algorithm initial populations that already have some general characteristics that are
known to be present in the optimal solution, e.g. the “hump” shape that was observed
in all the optimized spectral chirp distributions for the OH molecule in chapter 13.
The analyses performed in this thesis have served as a proof of concept to demon-
strate the usefulness of the genetic algorithm as a tool for investigating the principles
of quantum control. As a ﬁnal note, it should be stated that in real experimental
situations, variables such as unshaped pulse width and central pulse frequency are
not always controllable. These types of constraints are important to consider if the
173
==PAGE:184==
CHAPTER 15. GENERAL DISCUSSION
results of the theoretical work are to be truly useful in an experimental setting, and
should therefore be taken into account in future investigations.
174
==PAGE:185==
Chapter 16
Conclusion
16.1
Summing Up
The 4f-line pulse shaper was introduced and the principles of its operation were
described.
The concept of shaping an ultrashort laser pulse by applying a phase
chirp to the spectral distribution was introduced. This served as the motivation for
the analyses that were performed in the following chapters, in which techniques for
the mathematical description of femtosecond laser pulses were developed, and it was
shown how the Fourier transform can be used to transform these pulses between the
spectral and temporal domains.
A series of analytical and numerical analyses were performed in which the relation-
ship between the spectral phase chirp and the temporal pulse shape was investigated
for a number of diﬀerent spectral chirp functions. It was demonstrated how some
chirp functions like the quadratic chirp will produce temporal frequency chirp in the
shaped pulse. The Wigner distribution was introduced, and it was shown how it can
be used to visualize a pulse as a function of time and frequency simultaneously.
Having investigated the principles of spectral pulse shaping, the methodology for
simulating the interaction between a shaped pulse and a quantum system was de-
rived. This started with the introduction of the quantum wave packet and Erhenfests
175
==PAGE:186==
CHAPTER 16. CONCLUSION
theorem, which states that the wave packet expectation values will behave classically
in a potential that is of quadratic order or less. The analytic solution to a Gaussian
wave packet in a quantum forced harmonic oscillator was shown, and through this the
concept of harmonic excitation was introduced. Next, numerical methods for solving
the time-dependent Schrödinger equation were derived, and it was shown how the
Born-Oppenheimer approximation can be used to semi classically model a molecule
in a time-dependent electric ﬁeld.
Using these mathematical tools and the Morse potential model of the OH molecule,
a series of simulations were performed in which it was demonstrated that the anhar-
monic energy spacing of the Morse potential requires negative temporal frequency
chirp in the driving ﬁeld for eﬀective excitation. In this respect, the OH ground
state was driven by a series of laser pulses shaped by quadratic spectral chirps and it
was shown that the temporal chirp of these pulses is able to increase the dissociation
level at lower pulse energies, whereas higher pulse energies tended to produce more
dissociation for un-chirped pulses.
Next, the genetic algorithm was introduced as an optimization tool, at it was
demonstrated that it can eﬀectively predict the optimal spectral chirp distribution to
generate a pulse of a desired shape. To achieve this, the genetic algorithm was used
to ﬁt two diﬀerent representations of the spectral chirp distribution; A 9th order poly-
nomial chirp, and a discrete set of frequency bins. It was found that the polynomial
ﬁtting method converged faster than the discrete method, at the cost of an error rate
that increased when the optimal spectral chirp distribution became more complex.
The genetic algorithm was then used to generate pulses optimized to dissociate
the OH Morse potential molecule in two ways;
1. By optimizing a frequency function in the temporal domain, with a parameter-
ized trapezoidal pulse envelope envelope. It was found that a 4th order optimized
temporal frequency function was suﬃcient for maximizing the dissociation. It
was also found that varying the width of trapezoidal pulses with equal energies
176
==PAGE:187==
CHAPTER 16. CONCLUSION
in general did not impact the dissociation level after a certain minimum width
was reached.
2. By shaping the temporal pulse via optimized spectral phase chirp. It was found
that ﬁtting a quartic polynomial chirp function in the spectral domain was the
most computationally eﬃcient method, however the largest dissociation was
achieved by ﬁtting each discrete spectral component individually. It was also
found that varying the target width of the temporal pulse would inﬂuence the
dissociation level.
Finally, the genetic algorithm was used to generate pulses optimized to produce pop-
ulation transfer between energy minima in a double well potential model, using the
same techniques as before;
1. By optimizing a frequency function in the temporal domain, with a parameter-
ized trapezoidal pulse envelope envelope. Once again a 4th order function was
suﬃcient for optimal results. It was also discovered that there is an optimal
time window for the population transfer which appears to be mostly dependent
on the quantum system and less dependent on the parameters of the pulse.
2. By shaping the temporal pulse via optimized spectral phase chirp. Once again
the discrete spectral chirp optimization yielded the best performance. It was ob-
served that for successful transfers, the wave packet must remain well localized
throughout the process.
16.2
Final Remarks
The goals stated at the outset of this thesis have been met. While much ground
has been covered, there is much more that can be covered. Many of the results and
techniques explored herein have led to questions which, if explored, may one day lead
to new avenues of knowledge and insight.
177

imaginary component in the integrand. This means that the integrand will become
the envelope function. It can be shown that integrating any even function (besides
f(x) = 0) over symmetric integration limits will result in an asymmetric function :

APPENDIX D. CHIRP SYMMETRIES
D.3
Frequency Shifts
Applying an even chirp to the spectral distribution in equation6.3 can be interpreted
as adding a phase θ to the ﬁrst and third components:
f(t) = exp (i (ω0 −∆ω) t + iθ) + exp (iω0t) + exp (i (ω0 + ∆ω) t + iθ)
Now, consider the case where t assumes an arbitrary positive value t = ∆t, ∆t > 0
f(∆t) = exp (i (ω0 −∆ω) ∆t + iθ) + exp (iω0∆t) + exp (i (ω0 + ∆ω) ∆t + iθ)
f(∆t) = exp (iω0∆t −i∆ω∆t + iθ) + exp (iω0∆t) + exp (iω0∆t + i∆ω∆t + iθ)
f(∆t) = exp (iω0∆t) [exp (−i∆ω∆t + iθ) + 1 + exp (i∆ω∆t + iθ)]
f(∆t) = exp (iω0∆t) [2 exp (iθ) cos (∆ω∆t) + 1]
f(∆t) = exp (iω0∆t) [2 cos (∆ω∆t) cos (θ) + 2i sin (θ) cos (∆ω∆t) + 1]
f(∆t) = [cos (ω0∆t) + i sin (ω0∆t)] [2 cos (∆ω∆t) cos (θ) + 2i sin (θ) cos (∆ω∆t) + 1]
Taking the real part of this expression:
Re {f(∆t)} = [(cos (θ) cos (ω0∆t) −sin (θ) sin (ω0∆t)) 2 cos (∆ω∆t) + cos (ω0∆t)]
Re {f(∆t)} = [2 cos (∆ω∆t) cos (ω0∆t + θ) + cos (ω0∆t)]
Next, consider the case where t = −∆t:
f(−∆t) = exp (i (ω0 −∆ω) (−∆t) + iθ)+exp (iω0 (−∆t))+exp (i (ω0 + ∆ω) (−∆t) + iθ)
f(−∆t) = exp (−iω0∆t + i∆ω∆t + iθ)+exp (−iω0∆t)+exp (−iω0∆t −i∆ω∆t + iθ)
36
==PAGE:229==
APPENDIX D. CHIRP SYMMETRIES
f(−∆t) = exp (−iω0∆t) [exp (−i∆ω∆t + iθ) + 1 + exp (i∆ω∆t + iθ)]
f(−∆t) = exp (−iω0∆t) [exp (iθ) 2 cos (∆ω∆t) + 1]
f(−∆t) = exp (−iω0∆t) [2 cos (∆ω∆t) cos (θ) + 2i sin (θ) cos (∆ω∆t) + 1]
f(−∆t) = [cos (ω0∆t) −i sin (ω0∆t)] [2 cos (∆ω∆t) cos (θ) + 2i sin (θ) cos (∆ω∆t) + 1]
Once again taking the real part:
Re {f(−∆t)} = [(cos (θ) cos (ω0∆t) + sin (θ) sin (ω0∆t)) 2 cos (∆ω∆t) + cos (ω0∆t)]
Re {f(−∆t)} = [2 cos (∆ω∆t) cos (ω0∆t −θ) + cos (ω0∆t)]
Finally, directly comparing the case where t = ∆t and t = −∆t:
Re {f(∆t)} = [2 cos (∆ω∆t) cos (ω0∆t + θ) + cos (ω0∆t)]
Re {f(−∆t)} = [2 cos (∆ω∆t) cos (ω0∆t −θ) + cos (ω0∆t)]

Now it can be seen that when the potential V (q) is quadratic or less, the last term
in the above expression falls out, and the classical behavior is recovered. Since χ
is an expression for the packet width, it can also be seen that the relation can be
approximated as classical if the packet is very compact or if the time frame is very
short.

Appendix F
The Gaussian Wave packet
F.1
Free Particle
It can be informative to consider a wave packet with a Gaussian probability distri-
bution. The reason for this is that the Gaussian wave packet moving in a potential
that is quadratic or less is one of the few examples for which full, time dependent,
analytical solutions can be derived relatively easily! Starting with a simple example,
the general expression for a free-particle Gaussian wave packet at t = 0 is given by

Here it is seen that ∆x is a simple oscillating function when α0 ̸= a, and a constant

(F.3)
Inspection of the above expression leads to the conclusion that when α0 = a, ∆x (t)
becomes a constant in time. When these conditions are met, the wave packet is said to
be coherent, as it does not change shape. Figure F.2 shows an example of a coherent
wave packet. In this example, p0 = 0 and x0 = 1, which leads to the center of the
wave packet at xt to oscillate back and forth within the potential with frequency ω.
In the case where α0̸= a, the wave packet is said to be squeezed. The width of
a squeezed wave packet will oscillate with frequency ω. However, xt and pt remain
uninﬂuenced by variations in αt, i.e. the movement of the wave packet center is the
same for the squeezed and coherent cases. An example of a squeezed wave packet
is shown in ﬁgure F.3. Having introduced the concept of the squeezed and coherent
wave packet, it is time to focus on the γt term, which can be shown to yield two

From these expressions it can be seen that in the coherent case, γt will always be
real. In the context of equation F.1 this means that for the coherent case γt will
only contribute imaginary values to the exponential argument, i.e. the term will not
impact the evolution of |Ψ (x, t)|2 over time. However, in the squeezed case it can
be seen that γt can become imaginary.
This, in turn becomes a real part of the
exponential argument of equation F.1. The reason for this is that as αt changes the
width of the wave packet, the imaginary part of γt will ensure that the wave packet
remains normalized by adjusting the wave packet height.

an example of a wave packet with an initial negative impulse being deﬂected in the
opposite direction by the parabolic barrier of the potential.

Gobbler Boundary Reflection
imaginary potential [iEh]
-50

-10
0
Figure H.3
As was brieﬂy mentioned before, an imaginary “gobbler” potential at the simulation
boundary will absorb the wave function. In the Morse potential, the wave packet
has the possibility of becoming unbound and in a number of the later analyses, the
unbound wave packet will be a desired outcome.
Because of this, it is necessary
to place such a gobbler potential at the edge of the simulation space in order to
absorb any parts of the wave packet that reach this boundary and avoid undesired
interference. There are a few caveats to this method; to ensure complete absorbance,
the the proper form of the imaginary potential curve must be selected. For a curve
of a given shape, if the wave packet energy too low it may be reﬂected back in the
opposite direction when it reaches the boundary, as shown in ﬁgure H.3.
Likewise if the wave packet energy is too high, it may simply move through the
gobbler potential without being absorbed, as shown in ﬁgure H.4.
The goal is therefore to ﬁne-tune the slope and function of the gobbler potential
to the system energy until it absorbs as much of the wave packet as possible, as
illustrated in ﬁgure H.5.
79

==PAGE:273==
APPENDIX H. MORSE POTENTIAL
In practice, the optimal shape of the gobbler potential must often be found through
trial and error, since it can be hard to predict exactly what the spread of incoming
energy levels is going to be. The gobbler ﬁne tuning process performed in the OH
model involved driving the system to dissociation using temporally chirped frequencies
with varying amplitudes for t = 0 −T, after which the driving potential was turned
oﬀand the unperturbed system was propagated for an additional relaxation period
of Trelax = 50 [fs].
The dissociation probability was then plotted as a function of
time. Fluctuations in the dissociation probability when T ≤t ≤Trelax indicate that
the gobbler potential is reﬂecting some of the dissociated wave packet back into the
bound states of the system. By using this method of troubleshooting, the coeﬃcients
and width of the gobbler barrier and the width of the unbound region of the simulation
space were adjusted until these oscillations were minimized. The simulation space size
was chosen to run from R = 0 −14 [a0] and the gobbler potential was selected with
the following form:
Vgobbler = −0.01iΘ (R −14) (R −14)2
81
==PAGE:274==
Appendix I
The Genetic Algorithm
I.1
The ga() Algorithm
As stated earlier, the optimizations that will be discussed in the following chapters
have all made use of the Matlab ga() function.
In the following subsections, the
details behind Matlabs implementation of the genetic algorithm will be discussed.
Population
As described earlier, the population term describes the number of “individuals” that
are present in each generation. Generally, and in all implementations discussed in
this thesis, the population size will not change from one generation to the next (al-
though this is certainly a possibility for some implementations).
Matlabs default
population size is 200 individuals, when the number of free parameters is > 5, and 50
otherwise. As shall be seen, it was found that a much higher populations of∼5000
individuals were required for reliable convergence w.r.t. the optimizations performed
in the subsequent analyses. Unsurprisingly, there are many ways to construct the
initial population when performing a genetic optimization. In general, the closer the
parameters of the initial population are to optimal, the better the performance of
the algorithm. In the optimizations performed herein, the parameters of the initial
82
==PAGE:275==
APPENDIX I. THE GENETIC ALGORITHM
populations were randomly selected from within user speciﬁed parameter boundaries.
As can be imagined, it was found that selecting reasonable boundaries to the ini-
tial parameter distributions was an extremely important factor W.R.T. ﬁnding good
solutions. Because of this, the later chapters will include detailed discussions and
explanations of the reasoning behind the selection of initial parameter boundaries.
Children
Like most other genetic algorithms, the ga() function constructs the next generation
by creating three types of children from the individuals of the current generation:
• Elite children are selected from the best performing individuals of the current
generation. These individuals are directly passed on to the next generation with
no alteration.
• Crossover children are created by combining the genomes from two “parent”
individuals
• Mutation children are created by introducing random changes into the genome
of a single individual
As stated, the genetic algorithm will in general select individuals with higher highest
ﬁtness levels to create crossover and mutant children. The selection, crossover and
mutation processes are implemented stochastically. This means that performing two
subsequent optimizations will not always yield identical results, however it can be
assumed that if the algorithm parameters are properly optimized, the resulting values
should be fairly close to each other.
Child distribution
The default Matlab settings are that the top 5% of any generation will become elite
children. Of the remaining children, 80% will be crossover children, and 20% will be
83
==PAGE:276==
APPENDIX I. THE GENETIC ALGORITHM
mutation children. Having elite children ensures that the best performing individuals
in any generation will always be at least as good as the best performing individuals of
the previous generation. However, it has been found that too many elite children can
limit the ability of the algorithm to adequately explore the parameter space, which
is why the number of elite children in a population is by default set to be relatively
low. As shall be seen, it was often found that better results were achieved by setting
the elite number to even lower than 5% of the population size.
Parent selection
The method of selecting crossover and mutation children employed in all the optimiza-
tions performed herein is performed using Matlabs ”Stochastic Uniform(@selectionstochunif)”
function. For a selection of P parents/mutants from a population containing N indi-
viduals, the steps of this process are as follows:
1. Rank all N individuals according to their raw ﬁtness scores
2. Assign each individual a scaled ﬁtness value proportional to its rank. The scaled
ﬁtness function employed here is ﬁtness (Rn) =
1
√Rn, where Rn is the rank of
the nthindividual
3. Create a line of length L with N segments, where the length of the nth segment
is proportional to the scaled ﬁtness of the nth individual
4. Starting from the beginning of the line, take a step of random length l along the
line, where 0 ≤l < L
P . Select the individual that corresponds to this position
on the line as the ﬁrst selection of the P individuals that are to be selected
5. Select the remaining P −1 individuals by moving along the line with equally
spaced steps of length l, where l = L
P
Figures I.1a and I.1b show a graphical representation of the aforementioned process.
84
==PAGE:277==
APPENDIX I. THE GENETIC ALGORITHM
 Scaled Fitness Values
(a) After being ranked from highest to lowest,
the individuals in each generation are assigned a
scaled ﬁtness value where the ﬁtness of individual
n with rank Rn is ﬁtness (Rn) = 1/√Rn.
 Stochastic Uniform Selection
= selected individuals
scaled values line
selection spacing line
(b) After the individuals have been assigned their
scaled ﬁtness values, the selection of the crossover
and mutation children is performed using the
@selectionstochunif function.
First, a line of
length L with segment lengths proportional to the
scaled ﬁtness values is created. Selection of the P
Parents/mutants is then performed by taking an
initial random step linit where 0 ≤linit < L/P,
and moving along the line with step length L/P
until all P individuals are selected.
The result of this selection technique is that in general individuals with high ﬁtness
levels will be selected as parents, while at the same time ensuring genetic diversity in
the population by not letting the best performing individuals dominate the selection
process too much.
Crossover
To create crossover children, two parents are selected via the aforementioned selection
process and their “genes” i.e. the list of parameters that make up each individual are
randomly mixed with each other.
The default method used by Matlab, and the
method used here, is the Single Point(@crossoversinglepoint) function, which creates
a random bit string with a length equal to the number of parameters. The crossover
child is then created from the two parents by selecting a parameter value from parent
A if the corresponding bit in the string is a 1, and parent B if the corresponding bit
85
==PAGE:278==
APPENDIX I. THE GENETIC ALGORITHM
is a 0:
Parent A :[a, b, c, d, e, f, g]
Parent B :[1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 6, 7]
Random bit string :[1, 1, 0, 1, 0, 0, 1]
Child :[a, b, 3, d, 5, 6, g]
Mutation
The creating of mutation children is handled in two diﬀerent ways, depending on
whether or not the parameters of the solution are bounded. For unbounded problems,
the creation of a mutation child from a parent is achieved using the Gaussian(mutationgaussian)
function, which adds a gaussian distributed random number to each parameter of the
parent with mean value 0 and with the standard deviation of the kth generation given
by the following formula:
σk = σk−1

1 −Shrink
k
Generations

Where Generations is the speciﬁed maximum number of generations that the algo-
rithm will perform before stopping, which Matlab by default sets to
Generations = 100 × number of parameters
Shrink is a parameter that speciﬁes how the standard deviation changes as the number
of generations increase. By default, Matlab sets Shrink = 1. The standard deviation
of the ﬁrst generation is proportional to the size of the interval within the initial
population parameter boundaries (∆boundaries) and a constant factor called Scale:
σ1 = Scale × ∆boundaries
86
==PAGE:279==
APPENDIX I. THE GENETIC ALGORITHM
By default, Matlab sets Scale = 1.
For problems with parameter boundaries, the mutation is handled using the
Adaptive Feasible(mutationadaptfeasible) function, which creates random mutations
which are adaptive with respect to the success rate of the last generation, and chooses
step sizes and directions which ensure that any mutations will lay within the param-
eter bounds.
Constraints
A constrained optimization problem is one in which either the solution or the optimal
parameters must fall within some user speciﬁed boundary. This boundary can be
linear in nature, i.e. if the optimal parameters must lay within some interval. This
is relatively simple to handle, as the program will simply avoid testing solutions that
contain parameters that are outside of the speciﬁed limits. It becomes more compli-
cated when the boundaries are nonlinear. For example, in chapter 13, the parameters
of the one of the optimizations are coeﬃcients of a higher order polynomial function,
where the constraint is that the derivative of the function must always be positive.
In this thesis, the Matlab “penalty” algorithm is used to handle constrained optimiza-
tions. It works by assigning a penalty to solutions that violate the constraints, in the
following way:
• If the constraints are not violated, the individual is simply assigned a raw ﬁtness
value using the ﬁtness function
• If the constraints are violated, the individual is assigned a raw ﬁtness value
equal to the worst performing individual that does not violate the constraints,
plus a term that is equivalent to how much the constraints have been violated.
87

APPENDIX K. GENETIC PULSE OPTIMIZATIONS
K.2
Constraint Function
The ﬁrst thing that was considered in the design of the algorithm for producing
optimal spectral chirps was which characteristics were important to consider for the
temporal pulse. Due to the ﬁnite size of the time domain and periodic boundary
conditions of the DFT, it was found that simply allowing the genetic algorithm to
manipulate the spectral phase until an optimum result was found would result in
an unphysical temporal pulse shape, i.e. the pulse was so wide that the right side
of the pulse would interfere with the left side due to the periodic boundaries. One
possible solution to this issue is of course to simply increase the length of the region
around the input pulse. However, since the optimal pulse can be extremely broad,
the computational and memory workload involved to do this can become huge.
It was therefore decided that some kind of constrained optimization was required.
As explained earlier, Matlabs genetic algorithm implementation contains built in
linear and nonlinear constraint functions. However, these were found to be ineﬀective
for this particular purpose, perhaps due to the highly nonlinear relationship between
pulse widths and chirp functions, especially for discrete chirps distribution. Instead,
a more hands-on approach was implemented, which was as follows:
1. The minimum pulse width Wmin is calculated as the second moment width of
the un-chirped temporal pulse, as illustrated by the red curve in ﬁgure K.1a.
2. The maximum feasible pulse width Wmax for the length of the pulse region is
calculated by determining the second moment width of a completely ﬂat pulse
with a total energy equivalent to the input pulse, as illustrated by the blue
curve in K.1a.
3. A target width ∆T is chosen based on the region size. It is estimated to be of a
size such that the pulse will be suﬃciently localized in the center of the region
so that the interference due to periodic boundary conditions is minimized.
107
==PAGE:300==
APPENDIX K. GENETIC PULSE OPTIMIZATIONS

widths of the domain. Because a ﬂat-phase gaus-
sian pulse is Fourier limited, the minimum pulse
width is given by the width of the unshaped pulse.
The maximum width limits are estimated by de-
termining the second moment width of a com-
pletely ﬂat pulse with the same energy as the
unshaped pulse, as indicated by the blue lines.

Figure K.1
108
==PAGE:301==
APPENDIX K. GENETIC PULSE OPTIMIZATIONS
4. The target pulse location is chosen to be in the center of the time region at
t = 0. This will ensure that the outer edges of the pulse are as far from the
boundaries as possible. This means that the algorithm should try to ﬁnd a pulse
that maximizes the desired excitation of the system, and has a second moment
width where the right edge is as close to t = ∆T/2 as possible, and the left
edge is as close to t = −∆T/2 as possible. These locations can be expressed as
“target” locations for the left and right side of the transformed pulse:
T L = 0 −∆T
2
T R = 0 + ∆T
2
5. At each ﬁtness evaluation, the second moment width ∆P and center of the pulse
P 0 is calculated numerically. The locations of the right and left edges of the
shaped pulse can therefore be expressed as
P L = P 0 −∆P
2
P R = P 0 + ∆P
2
6. For a given location of the left edge of the shaped pulse, a maximum and
minimum feasible location of the right edge, P +
right and P −
right, respectively, is
calculated based on Wmin and Wmax:
P R
max = P L + Wmax
P R
min = P L + Wmin
7. Likewise, for a given location of the right edge of the shaped pulse, a maximum
and minimum feasible location of the left edge, P +
left and P −
left, respectively, is
109
==PAGE:302==
APPENDIX K. GENETIC PULSE OPTIMIZATIONS
calculated based on Wmin and Wmax:
P L
max = P R + Wmax
P L
min = P R + Wmin
8. The penalty factor for the left and right edge of the shaped pulse is then cal-
culated as an exponential function of the distance each edge is from the ideal
location, scaled by a factor α:
PenaltyL =







exp

(T L−P L)
(T L−P L
min) ln (α)

when T L −P L > 0
exp

(P L−T L)
(P L
max−T L) ln (α)

when T L −P L < 0
PenaltyR =







exp

(T R−P R)
(T R−P R
min) ln (α)

when T R −P R > 0
exp

(P R−T R)
(P R
max−T R) ln (α)

when T R −P R < 0
(As the equations indicate, when the width of the shaped pulse is equal to the
maximum or minimum feasible pulse width, the penalty functions will be = α.
This helps ensure that the “strength” of the penalty can be adjusted by simply
increasing or decreasing α.)
9. The penalized pulse ﬁtness (FitnessP) is calculated in terms of the unpenalized
pulse ﬁtness (Fitness):
FitnessP =
PenaltyL + PenaltyR
2

× Fitness
Where, as before, the ﬁtness is the inverse of the unbound probability, e.g.
Fitness =
1
Pdiss
110
==PAGE:303==
APPENDIX K. GENETIC PULSE OPTIMIZATIONS
The penalization factor will force the algorithm to ﬁnd an optimized shape that
is equal or close to the the desired width limits. The strength factor α has to be set
high enough to ensure that the algorithm does not ever opt for wider pulse, i.e. α
must be set so that the best performing pulse is always the one that has the desired
target width. In practice this requires some ﬁne tuning.
